2:14:643:0:14:72:379:694:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:A Portrait of the Artist as a Very Young or Very Old Innovator:
2:15:706:14:14:72:282:680:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:Creativity at the Extremes of the Life Cycle
2:16:750:14:14:72:167:666:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:David W. Galenson

2:20:813:14:30:273:338:610:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:ABSTRACT
2:21:821:30:24:72:539:580:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:Orson Wells made Citizen Kane, his greatest movie, when he was 25 years old; Frank Lloyd Wright
2:22:916:24:24:71:539:556:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:designed Fallingwater, his most famous house, when he was 70. Contrasts as great as this raise the
2:23:1014:24:24:71:539:532:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:question of whether there is a general explanation of when in their lives great innovators are most
2:24:1113:24:24:71:539:508:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:creative. For each of seven artistic disciplines, this paper examines a major innovation made by a
2:25:1211:24:24:71:539:484:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:very young artist, and another made by an old one, with the goal of understanding the role of the
2:26:1308:24:24:71:539:460:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:artist's age and experience in the accomplishment. The analysis shows why youth was necessary for
2:27:1405:24:24:71:539:436:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:the innovations of such conceptual artists as F. Scott Fitzgerald, Arthur Rimbaud, Maya Lin, and
2:28:1501:24:24:71:539:412:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:Orson Welles, all of whom produced their masterpieces before the age of 30, and why extensive
2:29:1594:24:24:71:540:388:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:experience  was  necessary  for  the  innovations  of  such  experimental  artists  as  Piet  Mondrian,
2:30:1697:24:24:71:540:364:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:Elizabeth Bishop, Henrik Ibsen, and Frank Lloyd Wright, all of whom made major contributions
2:31:1789:24:24:71:540:340:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:after the age of 60. This paper demonstrates the generality of the distinction between conceptual and
2:32:1890:24:24:71:539:316:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:experimental innovators in artistic disciplines, and the value of the analysis in explaining the very
2:33:1991:24:29:71:436:292:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:different relationships between age and creativity for the two types of artist.
2:34:2070:29:17:71:166:263:144:g_d0_f1:144:g_d0_f1:David W. Galenson

3:42:2207:13:14:144:465:671:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:As I believe no man a real poet or genius of any sort who does not 
3:43:2274:14:14:144:465:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:go on improving till eighty and over, I shall begin again and again 
3:44:2342:14:14:144:281:643:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:as often as you set me right. 
3:45:2372:14:14:144:420:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Robert            Browning            to            
3:46:2472:14:13:144:402:615:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                            Thomas            Carlyle,            18561 
3:47:2575:13:14:144:147:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
3:48:2575:14:14:144:468:588:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:You know, the great mystery that requires 20 years doesn’t exist in 
3:49:2643:14:14:144:192:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:any field. 
3:50:2654:14:14:144:390:560:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Orson            Welles,            19602 
3:51:2743:14:13:72:75:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
3:52:2743:13:28:72:533:533:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Recent research on important painters, poets, and novelists has established that there are 
3:53:2834:28:28:72:540:505:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:two different types of creativity, and that each is associated with a distinct life cycle of important 
3:54:2937:28:27:72:537:477:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:achievements.3 Conceptual innovators arrive suddenly at innovations based on new ideas. These 
3:55:3031:27:28:72:496:450:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:often constitute immediate and radical changes in basic conventions of their disciplines. 
3:56:3121:28:27:72:542:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Although these innovations can be made at any time, the most radical, and consequently the most 
3:57:3217:27:28:72:542:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:important, usually occur early in a career, when the artist has not yet become committed to habits 
3:58:3316:28:28:72:531:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of thought about the discipline, and is better able to perceive and appreciate extreme deviations 
3:59:3414:28:27:72:252:339:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:from existing methods and practices. 
3:60:3451:27:28:72:530:312:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In contrast, experimental innovators arrive gradually at innovations based more heavily 
3:61:3539:28:27:72:533:284:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:on perception. Their major innovations normally appear piecemeal in increments, which are the 
3:62:3633:27:28:72:532:257:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:results of an extended period of trial and error. The greatest experimental innovations are based 
3:63:3731:28:27:72:535:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:on long chains of experimentation, and therefore usually emerge only after many years of work, 
3:64:3826:27:28:72:190:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:late in an artist’s career. 
3:65:3854:28:28:72:539:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The purpose of this paper is to sharpen our understanding of this analysis by examining a 
3:66:3944:28:27:72:521:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:series of extreme examples of both types of creativity. These are drawn both from the artistic 
3:67:4039:27:0:72:502:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:disciplines that have already been considered in this regard and from others that have not 
4:68:4132:0:27:72:539:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:previously been studied. Examining extreme cases - of important innovations made very early or 
4:69:4227:27:28:72:502:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:very late in artists’ careers - can serve to spotlight the most basic differences between the 
4:70:4321:28:27:72:488:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:conceptual and experimental approaches. Doing this in a wide variety of activities can 
4:71:4408:27:28:72:519:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:furthermore underscore the most basic elements that are common to each of the two types of 
4:72:4499:28:28:72:129:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:innovation. 
4:73:4511:28:27:72:538:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The conceptual innovations considered here were all made by artists aged 31 or younger; 
4:74:4599:27:28:72:542:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:one was produced by a poet at the age of just 16. The experimental innovations studied here were 
4:75:4696:28:27:72:527:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:all made by artists aged 58 and above; two were made by artists above the age of 70. All these 
4:76:4791:27:28:72:490:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:innovations were among the most important works of very important members of their 
4:77:4874:28:28:72:492:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:disciplines, and consequently most rank among the most important contributions to the 
4:78:4960:28:27:72:539:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:respective disciplines in their times. They include what many literature critics consider the Great 
4:79:5060:27:28:72:524:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:American Novel, and what most film critics consider the Great American Movie - indeed, the 
4:80:5151:28:27:72:208:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Greatest Movie Ever Made. 
4:81:5177:27:28:72:540:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In every one of the following case studies, the goal is to understand the role of the artist’s 
4:82:5272:28:27:72:540:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:age, and career experience, in the accomplishment of the specific achievement selected. Thus for 
4:83:5369:27:28:72:538:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the conceptual innovators a basic question is how an artist so young can make such an important 
4:84:5465:28:28:72:532:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:contribution to a discipline: what characteristics of the innovation reflect the artist’s youth? For 
4:85:5566:28:27:72:535:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the experimental innovators, a basic question is why this late work is more important than most, 
4:86:5663:27:28:72:489:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:or all, of the artist’s earlier output: what about this innovation was aided by the artist’s 
4:87:5756:28:27:72:539:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:considerable experience? By asking these questions about these many individual masterpieces in 
4:88:5851:27:28:72:534:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:a diverse group of arts, we can gain greater insight into how age affects the production of major 
4:89:5949:28:0:72:158:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:artistic advances. 
5:90:5970:0:27:72:131:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fine Artists 
5:91:5983:27:14:144:431:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:If Picasso is the archetypal finder, who, then, is the seeker? 
5:92:6046:14:14:143:245:643:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Mondrian, no doubt. 
5:93:6066:14:14:143:402:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                David            Sylvester,            19954 
5:94:6158:14:13:144:448:615:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I couldn’t imagine a more banal activity than simply providing 
5:95:6221:13:14:143:269:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:visual kicks to the public. 
5:96:6249:14:14:143:395:588:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Joseph            Kosuth,            19705 
5:97:6339:14:14:144:147:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
5:98:6339:14:27:72:528:560:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Piet Mondrian arrived in New York in October, 1940, after the windows of his London 
5:99:6423:27:28:72:505:533:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:studio were broken and the house next to his was destroyed by German bombs. Mondrian 
5:100:6508:28:28:72:532:505:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:painted in New York for the next 3 ½ years, until his death in 1944. He spent much of that time 
5:101:6604:28:27:72:518:477:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:working on a group of paintings he had started in Paris and London and brought with him to 
5:102:6695:27:28:72:522:450:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:New York, and he consequently began and completed only three new paintings while in New 
5:103:6783:28:27:72:542:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:York. The last of these, Broadway Boogie-Woogie, which he completed in 1943, is reproduced in 
5:104:6877:27:28:72:527:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:more textbooks of art history than any other painting Mondrian executed in his career of more 
5:105:6971:28:28:72:536:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:than 50 years.6 Remarkably, therefore, the last painting Mondrian completed, at the age of 71, is 
5:106:7069:28:27:72:421:339:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:considered by art historians to be the most important one he ever made.  
5:107:7142:27:28:72:537:312:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Mondrian’s goals for his art were both vast and imprecise. He wrote extensively, if often 
5:108:7232:28:27:72:502:284:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:incomprehensibly, about the purposes of art and the means of achieving them. Both were 
5:109:7319:27:28:72:526:257:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:influenced by Mondrian’s belief in Theosophy. Carel Blotkamp explained that Mondrian took 
5:110:7409:28:27:72:522:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:from Theosophy the conviction “that all life is directed towards evolution, and that ... it is the 
5:111:7508:27:28:71:538:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:goal of art to give expression to that evolution.”7 A key turning point in Mondrian’s art occurred 
5:112:7607:28:28:72:525:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:around the time of his move from his native Holland to Paris in 1912, when his recognition of 
5:113:7701:28:27:72:521:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the importance of Cubism led him to believe that abstraction could present a purer version of 
5:114:7795:27:0:72:506:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reality, and consequently represent a more advanced stage of evolution. The aim of his art 
6:115:7888:0:27:72:530:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:became the creation of relationships between colors, and between shapes, that would transcend 
6:116:7982:27:28:72:129:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:naturalism: 
6:117:7994:28:14:144:428:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Pure abstract art becomes completely emancipated, free of 
6:118:8052:14:13:144:435:615:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:naturalistic appearances. It is no longer natural harmony but 
6:119:8114:13:14:144:441:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:creates equivalent relationships. The realization of equivalent 
6:120:8178:14:14:144:466:588:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:relationships is of the highest importance for life. Only in this way 
6:121:8248:14:14:144:429:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:can social and economic freedom, peace, and happiness be 
6:122:8305:14:14:144:196:560:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:achieved.8 
6:123:8315:14:13:144:147:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
6:124:8315:13:28:72:539:533:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Mondrian believed that artists could serve as leaders of society in moving toward these universal 
6:125:8413:28:28:71:526:505:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:goals. He recognized that the goals were distant, but he was patient: “he never rushed himself, 
6:126:8509:28:27:71:531:477:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:apparently convinced that evolution in art, as in the universe, was an extremely slow process.”9 
6:127:8605:27:28:71:539:450:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Mondrian’s working methods were explicitly based on intuition: “Pure intuition becomes 
6:128:8692:28:27:71:530:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:conscious through long culture and creates pure abstract art, which arises neither from intellect 
6:129:8790:27:28:71:529:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:nor from vague intuitive feeling.”10 Although his geometric compositions are often assumed to 
6:130:8884:28:28:72:398:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:be the result of calculation, he told a friend this was never the case: 
6:131:8956:28:13:144:444:339:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I believe that it is possible by means of horizontal and vertical 
6:132:9022:13:14:144:467:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:lines, created consciously but not calculatingly, guided by a higher 
6:133:9091:14:14:144:467:312:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:intuition and brought to harmony and rhythm...  to arrive at a work 
6:134:9159:14:14:144:459:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of art which is as strong as it is true ... And chance must be as far 
6:135:9229:14:13:144:270:284:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:removed as calculation.11 
6:136:9254:13:14:144:147:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
6:137:9254:14:28:72:532:257:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The artist Harry Holzman, who financed Mondrian’s immigration to the United States and later 
6:138:9347:28:27:72:298:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:became his heir, confirmed Mondrian’s claim: 
6:139:9392:27:14:144:455:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:There was no program, no symbols, no “geometry” or system of 
6:140:9453:14:14:144:426:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:measure; only intuition determined the total rhythm of the 
6:141:9512:14:14:144:466:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:relationships, by trial and error. The given space of the canvas, the 
6:142:9582:14:14:143:403:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:given tension of its proportion, its size, were likewise 
6:143:9639:14:13:144:447:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:experimentally determined and varied. Intuitive experience for 
6:144:9702:13:14:144:402:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Mondrian could only be direct, immediate, sensual.12 
6:145:9754:14:14:144:147:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
6:146:9754:14:0:72:535:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The criteria that guided Mondrian’s experimentation were aesthetic. An artist who knew 
7:147:9843:0:27:72:541:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Mondrian in Paris recalled watching him work in the 1920s: “if the black line was too thick, he’d 
7:148:9941:27:28:72:522:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:take a piece of white paper, or a paper of about the same color as the color planes next to that 
7:149:10038:28:27:72:542:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:line, and then he pasted it onto the canvas, and then held it at a distance to see ‘is the line the way 
7:150:10142:27:28:72:534:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I want it or not.’”13 Similarly, a friend from his last years in New York explained that Mondrian 
7:151:10240:28:28:72:532:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:proceeded visually: “He tests each picture over a long period by eye: it is a physical adjustment 
7:152:10338:28:27:72:343:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of proportion through training, intuition, and testing.”14  
7:153:10398:27:28:72:532:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Mondrian’s art developed gradually over decades of experimentation. John Golding has 
7:154:10483:28:27:72:514:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:pointed out that from an early stage of his career he worked in series, indicating that he was 
7:155:10578:27:28:72:541:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:concerned with themes rather than individual motifs.15 Michel Seuphor recalled that although the 
7:156:10675:28:28:72:508:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:differences between two paintings might appear insignificant to a layman, Mondrian often 
7:157:10764:28:27:72:522:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:considered the changes significant: “‘Even so, it’s another step,’ he once said to a friend who 
7:158:10860:27:28:72:542:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:was studying a new picture of his, ‘or don’t you think so? Don’t you find that it represents even a 
7:159:10960:28:27:72:540:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:little step forward?’”16 Mondrian’s dogged persistence made the cumulative effect of these many 
7:160:11056:27:28:72:513:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:little steps very great. As David Sylvester reflected, “A Mondrian retrospective is not just a 
7:161:11151:28:27:71:513:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:procession of great pictures, but a progression which in itself is an aesthetic experience: the 
7:162:11247:27:28:71:525:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:trajectory of the man’s art becomes as much a thing of beauty as the art.”17 The observation is 
7:163:11343:28:28:72:505:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:apt, for Mondrian’s lifelong quest came to have a goal more complex than simply making 
7:164:11430:28:27:72:532:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:images. A scholar who recently studied a group of paintings that Mondrian executed during the 
7:165:11524:27:28:72:541:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:last decade of his life concluded that “Mondrian’s painting practice in New York seems to reflect 
7:166:11622:28:27:72:528:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:an accelerating doubt about whether it is possible, or even desirable, ever to finish a painting... 
7:167:11722:27:28:72:536:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Mondrian had not abandoned the idea of a product, just redefined it as a discovery or a solution, 
7:168:11820:28:0:72:534:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not a painting.”18 Thus the painter Carl Holty wrote of visiting Mondrian’s studio in New York: 
8:169:11918:0:27:72:528:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“Watching the pictures change into others as he worked, I asked him whether he wasn’t losing 
8:170:12011:27:28:72:534:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:good pictures in numbers because of his exigence. He said, ‘I don’t want pictures. I just want to 
8:171:12109:28:27:72:165:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:find things out.’”19 
8:172:12129:27:28:72:498:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Mondrian’s method of constructing his paintings involved extended revision and 
8:173:12208:28:28:72:536:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:correction, due to his uncertainty, but the geometric shapes and limited palette of primary colors 
8:174:12307:28:27:72:496:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that made up his finished work are often taken to imply that his completed paintings are 
8:175:12396:27:28:71:501:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:impersonal and mechanical. So for example Clement Greenberg’s obituary of the painter 
8:176:12482:28:27:72:515:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:predicted that “Mondrian will be reproached for the anonymity with which he strove for the 
8:177:12573:27:28:72:540:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ruled precision of the geometer and the machine in executing his paintings: their conceptions can 
8:178:12671:28:28:72:512:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:be communicated by a set of specifications and dimensions, sight unseen, and realized by a 
8:179:12762:28:27:72:531:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:draftsman.”20 A number of artists and critics denied this, however. John Coplans observed that, 
8:180:12858:27:28:72:483:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:particularly in Mondrian’s late works, “a very pronounced brushwork is an important 
8:181:12942:28:27:72:541:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:compositional element of his painting.” Coplans contended that in fact the surface of Mondrian’s 
8:182:13039:27:28:72:470:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:paintings subtly expressed the uncertainty and doubt that lay at the core of his art:  
8:183:13126:28:14:144:456:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Perfection and imperfection lie side by side within his paintings, 
8:184:13193:14:13:144:453:284:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:mutually contradicting one another - the razor-sharp edge of the 
8:185:13258:13:14:144:459:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:black lines contrasted against the suffused surfaces. This conflict 
8:186:13326:14:14:144:439:257:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:between means and ends transmits a psychic tension... [T]his 
8:187:13387:14:14:144:452:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:duality serves as a check against ritualization and virtuosity and 
8:188:13454:14:14:144:442:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:enables his work to transmit the drama of search and struggle 
8:189:13516:14:13:144:311:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:without making a spectacle of it.21 
8:190:13551:13:14:143:146:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
8:191:13551:14:28:71:476:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Mondrian’s artistic goals, practices, and extended progress identify him as a 
8:192:13629:28:27:72:520:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:quintessentially experimental artist. The key question is how this helps us to understand why 
8:193:13723:27:28:72:532:133:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Broadway Boogie-Woogie emerges as his most important individual work. The answer appears 
8:194:13812:28:0:72:530:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to be that in this late painting Mondrian combined a number of elements that he had developed 
9:195:13908:0:27:72:531:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and painstakingly refined over the course of more than 30 years with several bold new devices. 
9:196:14003:27:28:72:540:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Meyer Schapiro pointed out the elements of Broadway Boogie-Woogie that Mondrian drew from 
9:197:14093:28:27:71:507:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:his own earlier work: “We see again the stabilizing grid; the molecular scattered units; the 
9:198:14186:27:28:71:537:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:repeated arrays of primary color as in his Neo-Impressionist phase; and the composition of large 
9:199:14283:28:28:71:503:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:squares applied as separate planes of color.”22 All these components had first appeared in 
9:200:14374:28:27:72:499:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Mondrian’s paintings between 1909 and 1917. To these long-established elements of his 
9:201:14460:27:28:72:535:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:vocabulary Mondrian then added significant new departures. Thus in Broadway Boogie-Woogie 
9:202:14549:28:27:72:537:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:he eliminated the network of black lines that had characterized his work since his first encounter 
9:203:14648:27:28:72:531:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:with Cubism. As he explained to a curator, in 1943 he had decided that his use of black lines to 
9:204:14745:28:28:72:525:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:define planes constituted drawing rather than painting: “In drawing, the lines are the principal 
9:205:14842:28:27:72:527:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:means of expression; in painting, the color planes.”23 At the same time Mondrian introduced a 
9:206:14936:27:28:71:511:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:new illusion of three-dimensionality into his work: “At certain crossings of the grid, he has 
9:207:15030:28:27:71:474:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:extended the color of the square unit to a neighboring unit of one or the other band. 
9:208:15116:27:28:72:542:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Distinguished by this accent, one band seems to come forward in crossing its perpendicular.”24 In 
9:209:15214:28:27:72:520:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:contrast to the stable, architectural effects of his paintings of the previous two decades, in his 
9:210:15313:27:28:72:505:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:great late paintings Mondrian achieved new depth, dynamism, and movement. This was a 
9:211:15398:28:28:72:541:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:product of the wisdom he had gained through four decades of research, based on both a profound 
9:212:15493:28:27:71:530:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:knowledge of his craft and an understanding of the value of the willingness to experiment with 
9:213:15588:27:28:72:514:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:bold new changes: “at the age of seventy-one he was quite prepared to begin his artistic life 
9:214:15682:28:27:71:539:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:anew.”25 Both his expertise and his openness to new approaches were direct consequences of the 
9:215:15777:27:28:72:320:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:experimental nature of Mondrian’s approach to art. 
9:216:15828:28:0:72:519:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In 1965, while he was a student at New York’s School of Visual Arts, Joseph Kosuth 
10:217:15914:0:27:72:534:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:made a work titled One and Three Chairs. It consists of a wooden folding chair, flanked on one 
10:218:16009:27:28:72:538:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:side by a life-size photograph of the same chair, and on the other by an enlarged photograph of a 
10:219:16107:28:27:72:527:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:dictionary definition of the word “chair.” Nearly four decades later, Kosuth is approaching the 
10:220:16203:27:28:72:534:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:age of 60, but One and Three Chairs remains by far his best-known work. A recent study found 
10:221:16296:28:28:72:529:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that it is in fact the single work made by an American artist during the 1960s that is most often 
10:222:16394:28:27:72:533:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reproduced in textbooks of art history.26 How did Joseph Kosuth produce a work this important 
10:223:16488:27:28:72:227:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:when he was just 20 years old?  
10:224:16520:28:27:72:537:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In 1964, Kosuth decided that painting was dead: “It was my belief that painting had been 
10:225:16609:27:28:72:532:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:dried up, used up.”27 He later explained that painting had served to enrich the visual experience 
10:226:16707:28:28:72:528:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of people who lived prior to the 20th century, but that the development of modern technology - 
10:227:16802:28:27:72:539:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:movies, television, and transportation - had made this function obsolete: “The visual experiences 
10:228:16900:27:28:72:469:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of the modern day man make paintings impotent and pathetic trophies to forgotten 
10:229:16981:28:27:72:524:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:aristocracies.”28 For Kosuth, Marcel Duchamp’s innovation of the unassisted readymade - the 
10:230:17073:27:28:71:506:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:presentation of unaltered manufactured objects as works of art - had decisively shifted the 
10:231:17165:28:27:72:310:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:emphasis of art from appearance to conception.29 
10:232:17213:27:28:72:540:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: As a 20-year-old art student, Kosuth consequently decided that being an artist necessarily 
10:233:17304:28:28:71:526:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:involved questioning the nature of art. This could not be done using traditional means: “If you 
10:234:17400:28:27:71:523:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:make paintings, you are already accepting (not questioning) the nature of art.”30 He needed to 
10:235:17495:27:28:72:505:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:find an alternative means that emphasized conception and ideas rather than execution and 
10:236:17584:28:27:72:535:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:objects: “the artist’s duty is really to present new ideas - new ideas about what art is - not just to 
10:237:17687:27:28:71:540:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:make new works within the accepted framework.”31 Part of Kosuth’s solution to this problem lay 
10:238:17782:28:0:72:524:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in the use of language: “I chose language for the ‘material’ of my work because it seemed the 
11:239:17879:0:27:72:502:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:only possibility with the potential for being a neutral non-material.”32 Another part of his 
11:240:17972:27:28:72:536:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:solution lay in using photography “as a non-art device as an alternative to painting within the art 
11:241:18072:28:27:72:515:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:context; I used photography in a way which was detached and indexed to the general use of 
11:242:18162:27:28:72:541:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:photography in the culture, rather than the craft of a ‘fine art’ of photography.” To emphasize the 
11:243:18262:28:28:72:542:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:insignificance of the craft of the photographic process, Kosuth never took his own photographs.33 
11:244:18359:28:27:72:533:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: One and Three Chairs was one of the first products of this combination of language and 
11:245:18446:27:28:72:514:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:photography. Each time it was exhibited, Kosuth would have a new photograph of the chair 
11:246:18535:28:27:71:517:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:taken in its new location. He explained that the change in the object was part of its meaning: 
11:247:18630:27:14:143:458:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I liked that the work itself was something other than simply what 
11:248:18696:14:14:143:465:450:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:you saw. By changing the location, the object, the photograph and 
11:249:18762:14:14:143:463:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:still having it remain the same work was very interesting. It meant 
11:250:18830:14:14:143:440:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that you could have an art work which was that idea of an art 
11:251:18892:14:13:143:451:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:work, and its formal components weren’t important. I felt I had 
11:252:18956:13:14:143:428:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:found a way to make art without formal components being 
11:253:19012:14:14:143:462:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:confused for an expressionist composition. The expression was in 
11:254:19077:14:14:143:465:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the idea, not the form - the forms were only a device in the service 
11:255:19146:14:14:143:208:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of the idea.34 
11:256:19160:14:13:144:147:339:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
11:257:19160:13:28:72:541:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Kosuth’s emphasis on avoiding “expressionist composition” places him squarely within a 
11:258:19247:28:27:72:531:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:movement that had begun a decade earlier, in which Jasper Johns, Robert Rauschenberg, Frank 
11:259:19339:27:28:72:503:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Stella, Andy Warhol, and others had sought to create new forms of art that eliminated the 
11:260:19429:28:28:72:508:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:emotional and psychological symbolism of Abstract Expressionism, which had dominated 
11:261:19514:28:27:72:525:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:American art in the late 1940s and early ‘50s. Yet One and Three Chairs was among the most 
11:262:19605:27:28:72:528:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:radical attempts of the time to move art beyond physical objects into a more purely conceptual 
11:263:19700:28:27:72:541:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:realm. In doing this, it struck a responsive chord with many young artists who wished to separate 
11:264:19798:27:28:72:518:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:art from the commercialism of the market. As Kosuth later recognized, his early innovations 
11:265:19890:28:0:72:532:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:were part of a growing artistic protest against American society: “It is impossible to understand 
12:266:19991:0:27:72:535:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:this without understanding the sixties, and appreciate [Conceptual art] for what it was: the art of 
12:267:20091:27:28:71:542:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the Vietnam war era.”35 Kosuth’s work quickly affected other artists, as both the role of language 
12:268:20190:28:27:72:476:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and the use of photography became central elements of many of the most important 
12:269:20271:27:28:72:335:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:developments in fine art in the late 1960s and beyond. 
12:270:20326:28:28:72:541:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: As Kosuth grew older, his views of art became somewhat more nuanced, and he qualified 
12:271:20412:28:27:72:516:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:his youthful categorical dismissal of painting. Thus for example at a symposium in 1982, he 
12:272:20504:27:28:72:502:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:explained that “Actually, when I first described [painting] as dead I was a kid - and I was 
12:273:20596:28:27:72:523:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:projecting into the future.”36 But in fact it was apparently the confidence and simplicity of his 
12:274:20694:27:28:72:516:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:youthful view of art that allowed him to make the radical departures from traditional artistic 
12:275:20789:28:28:72:532:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:practices that gained influence with other young artists who were seeking ways to present ideas 
12:276:20885:28:27:72:537:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:without making conventional art objects. That Kosuth could produce a contribution to fine art as 
12:277:20982:27:28:72:535:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:influential as One and Three Chairs at such an early age was a result of his success at creating a 
12:278:21081:28:27:72:529:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:form of art that embodied a complex idea without requiring the command of traditional artistic 
12:279:21176:27:28:72:388:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:methods and materials that required extensive study and practice. 
12:280:21242:28:27:72:120:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Novelists 
12:281:21251:27:14:144:465:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Yes, that was and ever is my greatest torment - I never can control 
12:282:21319:14:14:144:461:257:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:my material. Whenever I write a novel, I crowd it up with a lot of 
12:283:21386:14:14:144:465:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:separate stories and episodes; therefore the whole lacks proportion 
12:284:21454:14:14:144:213:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and harmony. 
12:285:21467:14:13:288:439:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fyodor Dostoevsky to Nikolay 
12:286:21496:13:14:288:421:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Strachov, April 23, 1871.37 
12:287:21523:14:14:72:75:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
12:288:21523:14:14:144:436:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I want to write something new, something extraordinary and 
12:289:21582:14:14:144:368:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:beautiful and simple and intricately patterned. 
12:290:21630:14:13:288:438:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:F. Scott Fitzgerald to Maxwell 
12:291:21661:13:14:288:461:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Perkins, July, 1922, about his plans 
12:292:21698:14:14:288:405:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:for The Great Gatsby.38 
12:293:21721:14:0:144:147:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
13:294:21724:0:27:72:510:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: There is widespread agreement with the judgment of Edward Wasiolek that “When 
13:295:21802:27:28:72:531:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Dostoevsky completed The Brothers Karamazov in November of 1880 - less than three months 
13:296:21891:28:27:72:537:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:before his death - he was at the height of his creative powers.”39 There is also general agreement 
13:297:21990:27:28:72:542:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that this final novel was Dostoevsky’s greatest achievement: Konstantin Mochulsky considered it 
13:298:22086:28:28:72:531:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to be “not only a synthesis of Dostoevsky’s creative work, but also the culmination of his life,” 
13:299:22184:28:27:72:532:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and Sigmund Freud declared that “The Brothers Karamazov is the most magnificent novel ever 
13:300:22275:27:28:72:125:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:written.”40 
13:301:22286:28:27:72:537:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Why Dostoevsky produced his greatest work at the age of 59, in the final year of his life, 
13:302:22377:27:28:72:532:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:can be understood by an artistic examination of his artistic goals and the methods he devised to 
13:303:22474:28:28:72:502:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:seek them. Dostoevsky devoted his art, and his life, to a pursuit of the unattainable: W. J. 
13:304:22567:28:27:72:513:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Leatherbarrow observed that for him “truth was infinite, commensurate with the wisdom of 
13:305:22656:27:28:72:516:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:God’s creation, and the search for it was an unfinalizable spiritual - not merely intellectual - 
13:306:22753:28:27:72:528:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:quest.”41 The great novels of Dostoevsky’s final two decades presented a series of innovations 
13:307:22848:27:28:72:458:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:aimed at communicating this belief, and at gaining a deeper understanding of it. 
13:308:22929:28:27:71:500:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Mikhail Bakhtin argued that in these late works “Dostoevsky is the creator of the 
13:309:23011:27:28:71:494:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:polyphonic novel. He created a fundamentally new novelistic genre.”42 This innovation 
13:310:23097:28:28:72:540:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:constituted a new way to represent the indeterminacy and uncertainty of human life.43 In place of 
13:311:23195:28:27:72:528:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the single controlling voice of earlier novelists, Dostoevsky created novels with “a plurality of 
13:312:23293:27:28:72:512:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:independent and unmerged voices and consciousnesses, a genuine polyphony of fully valid 
13:313:23381:28:27:72:533:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:voices.”44 One consequence of this was that no single character became the organizing focus of 
13:314:23476:27:28:72:518:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the novel: thus “the work seems to oscillate between several possible novels, each somehow 
13:315:23567:28:0:72:517:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:intended by a different character. It is this peculiar plurality that creates that special sense of 
14:316:23670:0:27:72:453:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:palpitating contradictoriness we recognize as quintessentially Dostoevskian.”45 
14:317:23749:27:28:72:541:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Dostoevsky believed in free will: as one scholar explained, he believed that  “the future is 
14:318:23842:28:27:72:533:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:unpredictable not only because of innumerable casual factors but also because the past does not 
14:319:23938:27:28:71:539:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:wholly determine the present. It shapes, but does not make, our choices.”46 He employed a series 
14:320:24035:28:28:72:516:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of devices to avoid a narrative structure that presented a linear sequence of events, and in so 
14:321:24131:28:27:72:494:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:doing made them appear inevitable. The polyphonic novel was a means of allowing the 
14:322:24215:27:28:71:540:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:characters of a novel “to be truly free, capable of surprising not only other characters but also the 
14:323:24317:28:27:71:539:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:author.” Dostoevsky’s creative process was “designed specifically to provoke unexpected events 
14:324:24412:27:28:71:526:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:or ideas, and the process would be a failure if it did not produce them.”47 Bakhtin approvingly 
14:325:24508:28:28:71:315:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:quoted the analysis of the critic Victor Shklovsky: 
14:326:24560:28:13:143:462:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fyodor Mikhailovich loved to jot down plans for things; he loved 
14:327:24625:13:14:143:463:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:even more to develop, mull over, and complicate his plans; he did 
14:328:24691:14:14:144:451:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not like to finish up a manuscript... As long as a work remained 
14:329:24756:14:14:143:470:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:multi-leveled and multi-voiced, as long as the people in it were still 
14:330:24827:14:14:143:461:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:arguing, then despair over the absence of a solution would not set 
14:331:24894:14:13:143:167:339:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in.48 
14:332:24899:13:14:144:147:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
14:333:24899:14:28:72:531:312:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Bakhtin observed that the same was true for individual characters: “As long as a person is alive 
14:334:24996:28:27:72:531:284:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:he lives by the fact that he is not yet finalized, that he has not yet uttered his ultimate word... In 
14:335:25099:27:28:72:522:257:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:[Dostoevsky’s] major heroes, ... the profound consciousness of their own unfinalizability and 
14:336:25193:28:27:71:542:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:indeterminacy is realized in very complex ways, by ideological thought, crime, or heroic deed.”49 
14:337:25290:27:28:72:539:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:In Bakhtin’s view, Dostoevsky’s plots were devised to make possible this open-endedness: “Plot 
14:338:25385:28:28:71:509:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in Dostoevsky is absolutely devoid of any sort of finalizing functions. Its goal is to place a 
14:339:25480:28:27:72:518:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:person in various situations that expose and provoke him, to bring people together and make 
14:340:25572:27:0:72:539:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:them collide in conflict - in such a way, however, that they do not remain within this area of plot-
15:341:25675:0:27:72:268:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:related contact but exceed its bounds.”50 
15:342:25716:27:28:72:533:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Gary Saul Morson has observed that many novelists are surprised by the actions of their 
15:343:25804:28:27:72:515:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:characters, as the authors’ initial plans are disrupted by the developing personalities of their 
15:344:25901:27:28:71:525:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:actors. Yet he noted that most authors then revise their manuscripts to hide their surprise from 
15:345:25998:28:28:71:485:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:readers, often by rewriting earlier sections so the characters’ actions no longer appear 
15:346:26087:28:27:71:540:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:inconsistent. Dostoevsky specifically avoided doing this, for he wanted readers to see that he had 
15:347:26186:27:28:71:485:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not determined his characters’ fate in advance.51 One way he did this was through the 
15:348:26272:28:27:72:512:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:serialization of his novels. He avoided planning his novels beyond the episodes that he was 
15:349:26364:27:28:72:524:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:currently writing. Once these were published, and unexpected developments appeared in later 
15:350:26456:28:28:72:526:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:installments, readers would see that the characters’ fate “was open or, rather, they had no fate. 
15:351:26554:28:27:71:541:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The work’s sections were to be understood as tending to no predetermined result, not as the mere 
15:352:26651:27:28:72:258:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:unfolding of a plan in several parts.”52 
15:353:26691:28:27:71:517:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: From at least the time of The Idiot, which he published in 1868, Dostoevsky avoided 
15:354:26775:27:28:72:538:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:predetermining the outcomes of his novels. Joseph Frank wrote that after Dostoevsky completed 
15:355:26869:28:27:72:518:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the first part of The Idiot, “it is clear from Dostoevsky’s notebooks and letters that he had no 
15:356:26966:27:28:72:528:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:satisfactory idea of how to continue the action. This uncertainty persists all through the middle 
15:357:27064:28:28:72:541:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sections of the book, ... where Dostoevsky is obviously writing from scene to scene with only the 
15:358:27162:28:27:72:514:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:loosest thread of any central narrative line.”53 As Shklovsky observed, “Dostoevsky’s plans 
15:359:27254:27:28:72:496:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:contain by their very nature an open-endedness which in effect refutes them as plans.”54 
15:360:27342:28:27:71:526:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Dostoevsky’s point was to communicate through the very composition of his novels the belief 
15:361:27434:27:28:71:540:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:declared by one of his characters in The Idiot: “It’s life that matters, nothing but life - the process 
15:362:27538:28:0:71:485:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of discovering, the everlasting and perpetual process, not the discovery itself at all.”55 
16:363:27631:0:27:72:499:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Dostoevsky was a quintessential experiment seeker: as Malcolm Jones observed, 
16:364:27709:27:28:72:519:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“Dostoevsky himself always insisted that the important thing was not the achievement of the 
16:365:27801:28:27:72:536:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:goal but the process of trying to reach it.”56 It is therefore not surprising that his greatest work is 
16:366:27905:27:28:72:539:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:valued for questions rather than answers: “The Brothers Karamazov matters not for its assertions 
16:367:28002:28:28:72:530:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and not for its denials but for its questions, to which there are no easy answers... Dostoevsky is 
16:368:28101:28:27:72:540:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:important because of the questions he asks about his society and about the beliefs held by men in 
16:369:28199:27:28:72:516:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:it, and above all because of his ability to dramatize the questions and show that they are real 
16:370:28295:28:27:72:113:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ones.”57 
16:371:28303:27:28:72:530:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: That this greatest work came at the end of Dostoevsky’s career was not an accident, for 
16:372:28391:28:28:72:525:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:its greatness was a product of both his increasing technical mastery of his craft and his greater 
16:373:28489:28:27:72:531:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:insight into the human condition. Thus Mochulsky describes his earlier books as the laboratory 
16:374:28584:27:28:72:531:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in which Dostoevsky developed the philosophical and artistic elements of Karamazov, with the 
16:375:28677:28:27:72:532:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ideology taken from Diary of a Writer, the structure of the family chronicle from A Raw Youth, 
16:376:28772:27:28:72:519:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the conflict between religious faith and disbelief from The Devils, and the scheme of a novel 
16:377:28866:28:27:72:519:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:about a major crime from The Idiot. Similarly, Mochulsky traces prototypes of Karamazov’s 
16:378:28955:27:28:72:530:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:central characters to the earlier novels.58 And beyond these literary survivals, Karamazov drew 
16:379:29051:28:28:72:529:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:on Dostoevsky’s personal struggles, not only his long reflection on religious belief but also his 
16:380:29149:28:27:72:536:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:experience of tragedy. Thus early in 1878 Dostoevsky’s work on Karamazov was interrupted by 
16:381:29241:27:28:72:532:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the death of his three-year-old son Alyosha. In an attempt to assuage the writer’s grief, his wife 
16:382:29340:28:27:72:513:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:convinced him to visit the monastery of Optina Pustyn, where he met with the elder, Father 
16:383:29431:27:28:72:531:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ambrosius. Dostoevsky used his vivid knowledge of a parent’s grief in his poignant account in 
16:384:29525:28:0:72:525:105:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Karamazov of the devastation of a peasant woman at the death of her child, and Dostoevsky’s 
17:385:29620:0:27:72:520:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:wife believed that the words Father Zosima used to console the woman in the novel were the 
17:386:29711:27:28:72:514:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:same ones that Father Ambrosius had asked Dostoevsky to convey to her in sending her his 
17:387:29800:28:27:71:484:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5:blessing after the death of their son.59 From his survey of the sources of The Brothers 
17:388:29888:27:28:72:496:602:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Karamazov, it is consequently not surprising that Mochulsky concludes that “spiritually 
17:389:29976:28:28:72:517:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:[Dostoevsky] had worked on it his entire life... Everything that he experienced, thought, and 
17:390:30070:28:27:72:297:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:created finds its place in this vast synthesis.”60 
17:391:30120:27:28:72:530:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: F. Scott Fitzgerald published The Great Gatsby in 1925, when he was just 29 years old. 
17:392:30207:28:27:71:540:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Although the book initially met with a mixed critical reception, and its sales greatly disappointed 
17:393:30307:27:28:71:522:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fitzgerald, since the writer’s premature death in 1940 Gatsby’s reputation has risen greatly.61 
17:394:30402:28:28:71:521:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Today not only is there a broad consensus that it is Fitzgerald’s best work, but many scholars 
17:395:30497:28:27:71:495:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:consider it to be one of the greatest novels ever written by an American.62 Over time, as 
17:396:30587:27:28:72:535:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:recognition of the magnitude of Fitzgerald’s achievement in Gatsby increased, the discontinuity 
17:397:30683:28:27:72:534:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:it represented in his career became clear. Thus in 1966 a scholar observed that “One of the most 
17:398:30780:27:28:72:504:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:difficult problems in Fitzgerald scholarship in the nineteen-fifties and sixties has been the 
17:399:30874:28:27:72:533:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5:attempt to explain the sudden maturing of Fitzgerald in 1925, with the publication of The Great 
17:400:30970:27:28:72:542:271:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Gatsby. Nothing in Fitzgerald’s earlier writing prepares for the authority and the aesthetic control 
17:401:31071:28:28:71:535:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:over material that is so impressive in his third novel.”63 Perhaps equally puzzling is Fitzgerald’s 
17:402:31171:28:27:72:539:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:subsequent failure to match the quality of Gatsby. Thus John Berryman observed that “Suddenly 
17:403:31265:27:28:71:538:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:he was able, not yet thirty, to lay out and execute a masterpiece. He was happily married, widely 
17:404:31363:28:27:71:497:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:admired, and had made money. One might have expected such a career of production as 
17:405:31447:27:28:71:379:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:American artists rarely have achieved. What happened then?”64 
17:406:31508:28:0:72:522:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Fitzgerald’s sudden rise and fall can be understood as consequences of the conceptual 
18:407:31597:0:27:72:542:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:basis of his best fiction, which used lyrical prose, simplified characters, and symbolic stage props 
18:408:31698:27:28:72:523:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and settings in the service of allegorical plots. So for example after Fitzgerald’s death, Lionel 
18:409:31796:28:27:72:511:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Trilling reflected that the significance of The Great Gatsby lay in the ingenuity of its form, 
18:410:31891:27:28:72:507:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:which derived from its “intellectual intensity.” He observed that the book’s characters and 
18:411:31983:28:28:72:429:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:settings were all simplified symbols used to serve the story’s central idea: 
18:412:32060:28:13:144:451:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the characters are not “developed”: the wealthy and brutal Tom 
18:413:32123:13:14:144:435:533:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Buchanan, haunted by his “scientific” vision of the doom of 
18:414:32183:14:14:144:467:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:civilization, the vaguely guilty, vaguely homosexual Jordan Baker, 
18:415:32250:14:14:144:438:505:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the dim Wolfsheim, who fixed the World Series of 1919, are 
18:416:32309:14:14:144:444:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:treated, we might say, as if they were ideographs, a method of 
18:417:32372:14:13:144:460:477:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:economy that is reinforced by the ideographic use that is made of 
18:418:32438:13:14:144:449:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the Washington Heights flat, the terrible “valley of ashes” seen 
18:419:32503:14:14:144:453:450:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:from the Long Island Railroad, Gatsby’s incoherent parties, and 
18:420:32567:14:14:144:402:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the huge sordid eyes of the oculist’s advertising sign. 
18:421:32623:14:14:144:147:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
18:422:32623:14:27:72:511:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5:Trilling added parenthetically: “It is a technique which gives the novel an affinity with The 
18:423:32717:27:28:72:520:381:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Waste Land.”65 T. S. Eliot himself appears to have appreciated the parallels between his own 
18:424:32810:28:27:72:491:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:poetry and the themes and techniques of Fitzgerald’s prose, for in 1925 he wrote to tell 
18:425:32899:27:28:72:509:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fitzgerald that Gatsby “excited me more than any new novel I have seen, either English or 
18:426:32989:28:27:72:531:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:American, for a number of years,” and that he considered it “the first step American fiction has 
18:427:33086:27:28:72:526:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:taken since Henry James.”66 More recently, Richard Ruland and Malcolm Bradbury described 
18:428:33175:28:28:72:542:243:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Gatsby as “a symbolist tragedy” told with “a symbolist mode of writing that informs everything - 
18:429:33272:28:27:71:408:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Gatsby’s dreams, parties, even his shirts - with an enchanted glow.”67 
18:430:33342:27:28:72:513:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Gatsby is elegant in both style and structure, and it is no surprise that lyric poets are 
18:431:33432:28:27:71:532:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:prominent among its admirers. When it was published Conrad Aiken called it “a highly colored 
18:432:33525:27:28:71:520:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and brilliant little novel which, by grace of one cardinal virtue, quite escapes the company of 
18:433:33621:28:0:71:521:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:most contemporary American fiction - it has excellence of form,” and two decades later John 
19:434:33716:0:27:72:540:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Berryman judged that “The Great Gatsby is in Chekhov’s sense a purely graceful book (‘When a 
19:435:33809:27:28:71:540:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:man spends the least possible number of movements over some definite action, that is grace’).”68 
19:436:33905:28:27:71:511:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fitzgerald’s own confidence in his accomplishment similarly serves to identify Gatsby as a 
19:437:33996:27:28:71:518:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:conceptual breakthrough, for when he completed the book he wrote to his editor “I think my 
19:438:34087:28:28:71:339:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:novel is about the best American novel ever written.”69 
19:439:34142:28:27:72:542:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The technical basis for Fitzgerald’s maturation in The Great Gatsby has been traced to his 
19:440:34233:27:28:72:538:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:newfound ability to distance himself from the story. Like his earlier books, Gatsby was based on 
19:441:34330:28:27:72:537:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fitzgerald’s own experiences and feelings, but in Gatsby he used two specific literary devices to 
19:442:34428:27:28:72:532:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:achieve greater objectivity. One was what he called “composite characterization,” as Fitzgerald 
19:443:34524:28:28:72:500:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:explained that Jay Gatsby “started as one man I knew and then changed into myself - the 
19:444:34612:28:27:72:539:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:amalgam was never complete in my mind.”70 The second, which Fitzgerald apparently borrowed 
19:445:34703:27:28:72:534:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:from Joseph Conrad, was his use of an observer, rather than the author, as the book’s narrator.71 
19:446:34800:28:27:72:522:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Both devices allowed Fitzgerald to attain a feeling of greater objectivity in Gatsby than in his 
19:447:34897:27:28:71:498:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:earlier novels, and thus to give a more universal significance to his own experiences and 
19:448:34987:28:27:71:115:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:feelings. 
19:449:34997:27:28:71:523:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The experience and feelings in question, however, were those of a young artist, and in 
19:450:35084:28:28:71:530:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:this lay the source of both the power of Fitzgerald’s achievement in Gatsby and his subsequent 
19:451:35179:28:27:71:537:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:inability to go beyond it.  Critics have long been aware that the strength of Fitzgerald’s vision in 
19:452:35280:27:28:71:528:188:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Gatsby came from its youthful simplicity and clarity. Lionel Trilling observed that “A writer’s 
19:453:35376:28:27:71:528:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:days must be bound each to each by his sense of his life, and Fitzgerald the undergraduate was 
19:454:35471:27:28:71:537:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:father of the best in the man and the novelist.” Trilling remarked that Fitzgerald was perhaps the 
19:455:35570:28:0:71:537:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:last significant writer to believe in the Romantic fantasy of heroism, and commented that “To us 
20:456:35670:0:27:72:526:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:it will no doubt come more and more to seem a merely boyish dream.”72 Alfred Kazin agreed, 
20:457:35761:27:28:72:527:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:noting that “Fitzgerald was a boy, the most startlingly gifted and self-destructive of all the lost 
20:458:35861:28:27:72:532:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:boys, to the end.” As such, he was able to create a small world, clearly perceived: “Fitzgerald’s 
20:459:35959:27:28:72:496:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:world is a little one, a superior boy’s world - precocious in its wisdom, precocious in its 
20:460:36051:28:28:72:127:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:tragedy.”73 
20:461:36062:28:27:71:515:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: But Fitzgerald could not retain the simplicity of his vision throughout all of even his 
20:462:36150:27:28:72:511:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:relatively brief life. John Aldridge recognized this when he wrote that “Gatsby was written 
20:463:36242:28:27:72:514:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:during that fragile moment when the drive of youth meets with the intuitive wisdom of first 
20:464:36334:27:28:72:491:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:maturity, and before either the diseases of youth or the waverings of age begin to show 
20:465:36422:28:28:72:538:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:through.”74 J. B. Priestley appears to have had this same phenomenon in mind when he analyzed 
20:466:36516:28:27:72:504:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:what he called the two contrasting and opposed strains within Fitzgerald. The first, which 
20:467:36607:27:28:72:502:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:dominated his early work, was that of the optimistic and romantic adolescent who passed 
20:468:36695:28:27:72:518:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:through Princeton into the apparently unlimited opportunities of the 1920s, believing his life 
20:469:36790:27:28:72:531:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:would always be charmed. The second strain, a detached and cool observer who gained control 
20:470:36882:28:27:72:515:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:as the romantic boy withered in the face of disappointments and failures, was an adult artist-
20:471:36976:27:28:72:521:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:priest dedicated to his craft but lacking in exuberance. Neither strain alone could produce the 
20:472:37072:28:28:72:526:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:highest art, and Priestley believed that Gatsby was Fitzgerald’s finest achievement “because it 
20:473:37168:28:27:72:529:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:seems to me to represent the one point of balance between these two conflicting strains in him, 
20:474:37264:27:28:72:397:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the brief period when they were able to collaborate successfully.”75 
20:475:37332:28:27:72:530:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Interestingly, Fitzgerald appears to have understood not only the distinction drawn here 
20:476:37421:27:28:72:533:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:between conceptual and experimental artists, but also the difference in their creative life cycles. 
20:477:37521:28:0:72:534:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Thus late in his life, in giving advice to his daughter about how she should prepare for a literary 
21:478:37624:0:27:72:526:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:career, he wrote that “The talent that matures early is usually of the poetic, which mine was in 
21:479:37721:27:28:72:505:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:large part. The prose talent depends on other factors - assimilation of material and careful 
21:480:37814:28:27:71:521:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:selection of it, or more bluntly: having something to say and an interesting, highly developed 
21:481:37909:27:28:72:529:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:way of saying it.”76 His own explanation of the declining quality of his work after Gatsby does 
21:482:38005:28:28:72:524:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not appear to have drawn on this analysis, however. Thus for example in 1929 he wrote to his 
21:483:38098:28:27:72:537:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:friend Ernest Hemingway that he believed that he had written too much in the five years prior to 
21:484:38195:27:28:72:524:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the publication of Gatsby, and that he had used up all his material: the three novels, dozens of 
21:485:38292:28:27:72:503:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:stories, and many articles and movie scripts “may have taken all I had to say too early.”77 
21:486:38383:27:28:71:310:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Elsewhere he offered a more poetic explanation:  
21:487:38432:28:14:143:434:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I have asked a lot of my emotions - one hundred and twenty 
21:488:38491:14:14:143:454:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:stories. The price was high, right up with Kipling, because there 
21:489:38557:14:13:143:447:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:was one little drop of something - not blood, not a tear, not my 
21:490:38622:13:14:143:458:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:seed, in every story, it was the extra I had. Now it has gone and I 
21:491:38690:14:14:143:258:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:am just like you now.78 
21:492:38713:14:14:144:147:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
21:493:38713:14:27:72:504:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:From the vantage point of the present study, however, Fitzgerald was unable to match the 
21:494:38802:27:28:72:542:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:achievement of The Great Gatsby in the last 15 years of his life neither because he lacked subject 
21:495:38901:28:27:72:537:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:matter nor because he had used up his stock of some magical emotional elixir of artistry. Instead 
21:496:38999:27:28:72:529:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the source of his artistic decline may simply have lain in the inevitable impact of accumulating 
21:497:39096:28:28:72:522:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:experience and aging, for the growth over time in the complexity of his perception of the real 
21:498:39191:28:27:72:531:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:world deprived him of the ability to recapture the simplicity and clarity of the allegorical world 
21:499:39290:27:28:72:207:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:he had created in his youth. 
21:500:39319:28:27:72:101:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Poets 
21:501:39324:27:14:144:454:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I accustomed myself to pure hallucination: I saw quite frankly a 
21:502:39389:14:14:144:456:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:mosque in place of a factory, a school for drummers attended by 
21:503:39453:14:0:144:438:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:angels, carriages on the roads in the sky, a living-room at the 
22:504:39520:0:13:144:330:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:bottom of a lake, monsters, mysteries. 
22:505:39559:13:14:144:408:671:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Arthur            Rimbaud,            187379 
22:506:39651:14:14:144:147:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
22:507:39651:14:14:144:448:643:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:No matter what theories one may have, I doubt very much that 
22:508:39712:14:14:144:449:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:they are in one’s mind at the moment of writing or that there is 
22:509:39777:14:13:144:366:615:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:even a physical possibility that they could be. 
22:510:39825:13:14:144:411:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Elizabeth            Bishop,            195080 
22:511:39919:14:14:144:147:588:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
22:512:39919:14:28:72:538:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: André Breton, the poet and founder of Surrealism, considered Arthur Rimbaud the father 
22:513:40006:28:27:72:537:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of modern poetry.81 Remarkably, Rimbaud completed his career as a poet at the age of 19, when 
22:514:40100:27:28:72:534:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:he renounced literature; although he lived another 18 years, until his death at 37, he never again 
22:515:40199:28:27:72:538:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:wrote verse. Rimbaud had written what would become his most famous poem, “Le Bateau ivre,” 
22:516:40290:27:28:72:531:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in 1871, when he was 16.82 A recent scholar called this “the first great Symbolist poem,” and it 
22:517:40387:28:28:71:499:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:has received considerable attention from poets as well as critics - among those who have 
22:518:40476:28:27:71:530:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:translated it into English, for example, are Samuel Beckett and Robert Lowell.83 These striking 
22:519:40572:27:28:72:532:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:facts about Rimbaud’s remarkable career dramatically raise the question of how he could make 
22:520:40665:28:27:72:380:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:so great an achievement so young, in such a brief span of time.  
22:521:40730:27:28:72:542:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Rimbaud grew up in the provincial town of Charleville, in northeastern France. He had an 
22:522:40819:28:27:72:513:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:unhappy childhood, and by his own account took refuge in imaginary adventures. He was a 
22:523:40907:27:28:72:536:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:brilliant student, and became a rebellious adolescent. At 15, with the encouragement of Georges 
22:524:41003:28:28:72:530:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Izambard, a young poet who was one of his teachers, Rimbaud began to write poetry. The next 
22:525:41095:28:27:72:527:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:year Rimbaud wrote two letters, one to Izambard, the other to another young poet, which have 
22:526:41188:27:28:72:529:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:become known as the “Lettres du voyant.” In them Rimbaud proposed nothing less than a new 
22:527:41278:28:27:72:487:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:theory of poetry. Edmund Wilson observed that this theory, “though more violent and 
22:528:41362:27:28:72:539:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:apocalyptic than most expressions of Symbolist doctrine, prophesied the advent of Symbolism.”  
22:529:41457:28:0:72:515:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Rimbaud believed that “one must be a visionary - one must make oneself a visionary.” This 
23:530:41550:0:27:72:525:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:would be achieved systematically through hallucination: “The poet makes himself a visionary 
23:531:41641:27:28:71:515:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:through a long, immense and reasoned derangement of all the senses.”84 By suppressing his 
23:532:41731:28:27:72:530:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:conscious senses, the poet would find a new voice. Thus Rimbaud declared that “It is wrong to 
23:533:41825:27:28:72:542:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:say: I think. One ought to say: I am being thought. I is Another.” Rimbaud effectively wanted the 
23:534:41923:28:28:72:538:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:poet to become the medium of an oracle, but rather than a divine oracle that spoke from without, 
23:535:42020:28:27:72:500:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:his goal was to find an oracle that came from within himself, the voice of the poet’s own 
23:536:42110:27:28:72:150:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:subconscious.85 
23:537:42125:28:27:72:516:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: “Le Bateau ivre” was the first major work based on Rimbaud’s new theory. Wallace 
23:538:42206:27:28:72:524:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Fowlie observed that it is “not only a successful poem in itself, it is the archetype of all future 
23:539:42306:28:28:72:541:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:work. It contains his principal themes, the order of his experiences, the evolution of his work and 
23:540:42406:28:27:72:504:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:his poetics.”86 The language of the poem literally describes the voyage of a drunken boat, 
23:541:42497:27:28:72:532:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:narrated by the boat, describing both its adventures and emotions, as it passes from servitude to 
23:542:42595:28:27:72:531:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:liberty, and finally to debilitation and disintegration. The poem is obviously allegorical, but the 
23:543:42695:27:28:72:479:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:symbolism is never clearly identified, and as a result there have been many different 
23:544:42781:28:27:72:529:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:interpretations of the poem.87 Like Rimbaud’s other poetry, “Le Bateau ivre” uses esoteric and 
23:545:42876:27:28:72:352:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:inventive language to produce vivid and complex images. 
23:546:42932:28:28:72:542:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The greatest literary influence on “Le bateau ivre” was Charles Baudelaire, who Rimbaud 
23:547:43020:28:27:72:541:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:considered “the first seer, king of poets.”88 Yet Marjorie Perloff has argued that Rimbaud made a 
23:548:43118:27:28:72:500:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:key departure from Baudelaire. Whereas the symbolism of Baudelaire’s poetry remained 
23:549:43203:28:27:72:503:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:anchored to his perception of external reality, Rimbaud’s “Le Bateau ivre” was no longer 
23:550:43292:27:28:72:510:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:concerned with providing links between his images and the outside world, but instead with 
23:551:43382:28:0:71:510:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:recording the processes of exploration of his own consciousness. This would constitute the 
24:552:43476:0:27:72:542:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:beginning of a powerful force, not only in modern poetry but in the arts in general, by creating an 
24:553:43576:27:28:72:540:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:art based not on perception but on conception. The artists who would follow this practice include 
24:554:43674:28:27:72:535:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not only many Symbolist poets but also many leading painters of the twentieth century: thus for 
24:555:43770:27:28:72:506:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:example Picasso declared that he painted not what he saw, but what he knew based on his 
24:556:43858:28:28:72:530:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:acquaintance with specific real objects. Yet Rimbaud’s departure was even more radical, for in 
24:557:43953:28:27:72:525:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:presenting directly the products of the poet’s subconscious Perloff observed that he advocated 
24:558:44048:27:28:72:506:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:using the poem “as language construction in which the free play of possible significations 
24:559:44139:28:27:72:538:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:replaces iconic representation.”89 Thus, perhaps for the first time, Rimbaud produced modern art 
24:560:44236:27:28:72:480:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that was not based in any way on representation of actual or even possible objects or 
24:561:44322:28:28:72:528:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:experiences. Rimbaud’s belief in the autonomous use of language and images accounts for the 
24:562:44414:28:27:72:501:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:enigmatic nature of the symbolism of “Le Bateau ivre,” and the many contending critical 
24:563:44502:27:28:71:526:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:readings of the poem. It also accounts for Rimbaud’s choice of an ocean voyage as the central 
24:564:44596:28:27:71:402:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:subject of the poem despite the fact that he had never seen an ocean. 
24:565:44666:27:28:71:527:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The importance of “Le Bateau ivre” thus appears to derive in large part from its role as 
24:566:44755:28:27:71:521:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the first embodiment of a radical new theory of poetry, a work executed by a self-proclaimed 
24:567:44848:27:28:71:523:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:prophet whose goal was not only to revolutionize poetry, but to inspire other poets to become 
24:568:44942:28:28:72:539:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:visionaries and change society.90 Anna Balakian has attributed Rimbaud’s ability to make such a 
24:569:45038:28:27:72:539:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:bold departure specifically to his youth: “At heart, the deviation that his poetry represents is akin 
24:570:45140:27:28:72:535:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to the sensations experienced in childhood. Every child in his dreams plays that delightful game 
24:571:45237:28:27:72:542:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of creating a world which is as far removed from this one as possible, a world that becomes more 
24:572:45334:27:28:72:512:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and more absurd as his efforts to pass the limits of the known are increased.” For Balakian, 
24:573:45427:28:0:72:524:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Rimbaud’s poetry can be explained as a peculiar anomaly: “by a miracle - that imagination of 
25:574:45523:0:27:72:527:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:childhood, somewhat prolonged in him, was still his when he developed the philosophical and 
25:575:45615:27:28:72:541:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:verbal power which should have come much later, after that excessively imaginative impulse had 
25:576:45710:28:27:72:509:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:been attenuated.” This unlikely juxtaposition created in Rimbaud’s poetry “the only image 
25:577:45800:27:28:72:539:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:perhaps ever recorded of the world as seen through children’s eyes, a world wholly incompatible 
25:578:45896:28:28:72:197:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:with the age of reason.”91 
25:579:45922:28:27:72:519:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: After retiring from literature at 19, Rimbaud went on to a career as an adventurer and 
25:580:46009:27:28:72:537:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sometime African trader in a variety of commodities, including contraband guns and slaves, that 
25:581:46105:28:27:72:528:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:would make him an inspiration for twentieth-century artistic rebels ranging from Henry Miller 
25:582:46199:27:28:72:509:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and William Burroughs to Bob Dylan and Jim Morrison. More remarkable than these later 
25:583:46285:28:28:72:500:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:escapades, however, was his ability to create new poetic forms, that would inspire major 
25:584:46374:28:27:72:530:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:twentieth-century movements in poetry and other arts, between the ages of just 16 and 19. This 
25:585:46469:27:28:72:536:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ability was a direct consequence of the radically conceptual nature of his work, which combined 
25:586:46565:28:27:72:386:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the vivid imagination of a child with the verbal skills of an adult. 
25:587:46634:27:28:72:534:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Elizabeth Bishop published her first poems in a high school magazine at the age of 14.92 
25:588:46722:28:27:71:536:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Unlike Rimbaud, however, she continued to write verse in her 20s, and well beyond. In 1976, at 
25:589:46817:27:28:71:533:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the age of 65, she published “One Art,” which is the second most frequently anthologized of all 
25:590:46913:28:28:71:135:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:her poems.93 
25:591:46925:28:27:72:523:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Bishop’s poetry is celebrated for its visual qualities. Randall Jarrell wrote that “all her 
25:592:47017:27:28:72:529:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:poems have written underneath, I have seen it,” and Robert Lowell declared in 1969 that “I am 
25:593:47111:28:27:72:540:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sure no living poet is as curious and observant as Miss Bishop.”94 Bishop herself told a critic that 
25:594:47212:27:28:71:530:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“the settings, or descriptions, of my poems are almost invariably just plain facts - or as close to 
25:595:47312:28:0:71:518:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the facts as I can write them.”95 Her poems were crafted thoughtfully and painstakingly. In a 
26:596:47409:0:27:72:528:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:1947 review, Lowell described her poems as “unrhetorical, cool, and beautifully thought-out,” 
26:597:47503:27:28:72:516:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and called Bishop “one of the best craftsmen alive.”96 In a poem written for Bishop in 1973, 
26:598:47596:28:27:72:528:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Lowell compared her to the experimental painter Albert Ryder: “His painting was repainting, / 
26:599:47690:27:28:72:534:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:his tiniest work weighs heavy in the hand.” Ryder’s endless revision of his paintings, which left 
26:600:47788:28:28:72:512:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:them heavy from accumulated layers of paint, was Lowell’s analogy for Bishop’s extended 
26:601:47876:28:27:72:530:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:searches for just the right words to complete her poems: “Do / you still hang your words in air, 
26:602:47973:27:28:72:528:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ten years /  unfinished, glued to your notice board, with gaps / or empties for the unimaginable 
26:603:48070:28:27:72:122:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:phrase.”97 
26:604:48080:27:28:72:531:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Another friend of Bishop’s, Marianne Moore, praised her poetry for its understatement: 
26:605:48167:28:28:72:525:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“With poetry as with homilectics, tentativeness can be more positive than positiveness, and in 
26:606:48262:28:27:72:507:408:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:North & South [1946] a much instructed persuasiveness is emphasized by uninsistence.”98 
26:607:48349:27:28:71:521:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Bishop’s own statements of her goals often reflected her diffidence, as for example when she 
26:608:48442:28:27:71:542:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:told an interviewer that “I always try to stick as much as possible to what really happened when I 
26:609:48541:27:28:71:234:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:describe something in a poem.”99 
26:610:48573:28:27:72:532:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The descriptive intent of her poetry, its visual nature, her careful craftsmanship, and her 
26:611:48665:27:28:72:507:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:tentativeness all identify Bishop as an experimental artist. A biographer recognized this in
