26:612:48758:28:28:72:488:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:observing that Bishop was “a poet whose method was her message.”100 Bishop herself 
26:613:48841:28:27:72:532:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:emphasized that poetry was not merely a part of her life, but her way of living: “Writing poetry 
26:614:48938:27:28:72:523:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:is a way of life, not a matter of testifying but of experiencing. It is not the way one goes about 
26:615:49037:28:27:72:366:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:interpreting the world, but the very process of sensing it.”101  
26:616:49102:27:28:72:532:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Like other great experimental artists, Bishop developed her art gradually. In spite of her 
26:617:49193:28:0:72:535:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:instinctual reticence, over time her poetry became more personal, and drew more heavily on her 
27:618:49291:0:27:72:534:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:own thoughts and experiences.102 Reading the openly autobiographical poems her friend Robert 
27:619:49384:27:28:72:529:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Lowell published in his landmark book Life Studies was one of the events that prompted her to 
27:620:49478:28:27:72:528:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reconsider her own work.103 Thus in 1957, when Bishop was 46, in a letter to Lowell about his 
27:621:49572:27:28:72:533:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:new work she expressed her admiration for his achievement and frustration at her own timidity: 
27:622:49667:28:28:72:539:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“Oh heavens, when does one begin to write the real poems? I certainly feel as if I never had. But 
27:623:49765:28:27:72:532:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of course I don’t feel that way about yours - they all seem real as real - and getting more so.”104 
27:624:49864:27:28:71:540:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Gradually thereafter Bishop’s own poetry became more autobiographical, and more assured. In a 
27:625:49958:28:27:71:528:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:review of a new volume of her poems in 1969, John Ashbery observed that Bishop’s work had 
27:626:50048:27:28:71:218:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:recently gained new maturity: 
27:627:50078:28:14:143:458:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Perhaps some of the urgency of the North & South poems [1946] 
27:628:50140:14:14:143:454:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:has gone, but this is more than compensated by the calm control 
27:629:50204:14:13:143:460:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:she now commands. Where she sometimes seemed nervous... she 
27:630:50264:13:14:143:435:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:now is easy in a way that increased knowledge and maturity 
27:631:50323:14:14:143:188:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:allow.105 
27:632:50332:14:14:144:147:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
27:633:50332:14:27:72:536:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Similarly, in a more recent review of Bishop’s career, Jacqueline Vaught Brogan concluded that 
27:634:50427:27:28:71:420:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“Bishop’s verse matured as she did, so that her late work is her best.”106 
27:635:50501:28:27:72:530:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: “One Art” appeared in the last of Bishop’s books published during her lifetime. It takes 
27:636:50590:27:28:72:538:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the form of a villanelle, a 16th-century form made up of a series of three-lined stanzas and a final 
27:637:50691:28:28:72:504:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:quatrain, in which the first and third lines of the first stanza are repeated alternately in the 
27:638:50788:28:27:72:518:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:succeeding stanzas as a refrain, and together form a final couplet in the quatrain. Written ten 
27:639:50884:27:28:72:537:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:years after the suicide of Lota de Macedo Soares, who had been Bishop’s lover for more than 15 
27:640:50979:28:27:72:542:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:years, in “One Art” Bishop expressed both the enormity of her loss and her belief that the need to 
27:641:51078:27:28:72:494:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:write about it was the greatest source of her ability to bear that loss. Beginning with the 
27:642:51170:28:0:72:541:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:apparently innocuous statement that “The art of losing isn’t hard to master,” the poem effectively 
28:643:51272:0:27:72:540:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:portrays life as a series of losses, which grow as the poem proceeds, from trivial household items 
28:644:51371:27:28:72:511:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to mementos, to houses, to cities, to continents, and finally to a loved one. The irony of the 
28:645:51466:28:27:72:528:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:opening statement grows with each repetition, as the increasing magnitude of the losses makes 
28:646:51560:27:28:72:524:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:them more and more painful to the author. The contrast between the rigid requirements of the 
28:647:51653:28:28:72:532:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:villanelle form and the poem’s intensely emotional subject serves to heighten the sense of pain, 
28:648:51750:28:27:72:520:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:for as the poem progresses the reader becomes increasingly aware of the enormous effort the 
28:649:51842:27:28:72:525:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:author is making to hold her emotions in check.107 In the poem’s final line Bishop violates the 
28:650:51938:28:27:71:520:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:villanelle’s strict form, interrupting the final refrain with a parenthetic instruction to herself - 
28:651:52039:27:28:71:521:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“(Write it!)” - that underscores her recognition that it is only in producing her art that she can 
28:652:52138:28:28:71:512:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:endure her greatest loss. Bishop’s papers contain no less than 17 drafts of “One Art,” in the 
28:653:52232:28:27:71:480:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:course of which she not only searched for the right words, but carefully explored her 
28:654:52318:27:28:71:512:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:emotions.108 The poem reveals a mature artist subtly adapting a complex poetic form to her 
28:655:52409:28:27:71:540:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:purposes, firmly using the skill she has acquired over decades to help her understand and survive 
28:656:52507:27:28:71:528:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:her pain. The title of the poem implies that for Bishop her poetry and her life were one and the 
28:657:52604:28:27:72:103:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:same. 
28:658:52610:27:28:72:537:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In a eulogy for a friend, Elizabeth Bishop wrote: “There are some people whom we envy 
28:659:52696:28:28:72:525:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not because they are rich or handsome or successful, although they may be any or all of these, 
28:660:52791:28:27:72:532:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:but because everything they are and do seems to be all of a piece, so that even if they wanted to 
28:661:52889:27:28:72:541:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:they could not be or do otherwise.”109 The observation applies equally to Bishop herself, with the 
28:662:52988:28:27:72:522:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:integration of her life and art, and her consistent efforts to achieve “clarity and simplicity.”110 
28:663:53087:27:28:72:526:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The greatness of her late work was a consequence of her increasing technical mastery, born of 
28:664:53181:28:0:72:536:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:years of experimentation, combined not only with a growing understanding of her emotions, but 
29:665:53278:0:27:72:396:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:also a growing willingness to express that understanding in her art. 
29:666:53347:27:28:72:120:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Sculptors 
29:667:53356:28:14:144:382:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:The only principle in art is to copy what you see. 
29:668:53407:14:13:144:375:615:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Auguste            Rodin111 
29:669:53482:13:14:144:470:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:We must take the object which we wish to create and begin with its 
29:670:53549:14:14:144:469:588:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:central core. In this way we shall uncover new laws and new forms 
29:671:53615:14:14:143:440:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:which link it invisibly but mathematically to an EXTERNAL 
29:672:53673:14:14:143:469:560:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:PLASTIC INFINITY and to an INTERNAL PLASTIC INFINITY. 
29:673:53727:14:13:288:433:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5:Umberto Boccioni, Technical 
29:674:53754:13:14:288:445:533:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Manifesto of Futurist Sculpture, 
29:675:53787:14:14:288:327:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:1912112 
29:676:53794:14:14:144:147:505:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
29:677:53794:14:27:72:489:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In 1891 Emile Zola, as president of the Société des Gens de Lettres, awarded a 
29:678:53873:27:28:72:535:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:commission to Auguste Rodin for a sculpture of the novelist Honoré de Balzac, who had been a 
29:679:53966:28:28:72:537:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5:founder of the literary society. When Rodin finally completed a plaster version of his Monument 
29:680:54062:28:27:71:516:408:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:to Balzac, seven years later, he was 58 years old. He considered the sculpture to be his most 
29:681:54156:27:28:71:526:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:important work, "the sum of my whole life, result of a lifetime of effort, the mainspring of my 
29:682:54252:28:27:71:537:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:aesthetic theory."113 Yet when the sculpture was exhibited at the Salon of 1898 it caused a storm 
29:683:54350:27:28:72:541:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of protest by critics. The Société des Gens de Lettres voted to dishonor its contract and refuse the 
29:684:54451:28:27:72:518:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:statue, and a group of young artists actually plotted to vandalize it.114 Stung by the criticism, 
29:685:54549:27:28:72:532:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Rodin withdrew Balzac from the Salon, and moved it to his home outside Paris. The statue was 
29:686:54642:28:28:71:495:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not cast in bronze until 1939, more than two decades after Rodin's death. George Heard 
29:687:54729:28:27:71:530:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Hamilton observed of Balzac that “the true originality of the work was reflected in the fact that 
29:688:54827:27:28:71:514:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:there were no proper words to confine it;” Alfred Barr described it as “one of the very great 
29:689:54921:28:27:71:315:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sculptures in the entire history of Western art.”115  
29:690:54975:27:28:72:533:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Rodin's career developed slowly. His early failure in three attempts to gain admission to 
29:691:55065:28:0:72:530:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the prestigious Ecole des Beaux Arts caused him to spend nearly two decades as an assistant to 
30:692:55163:0:27:72:541:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:other sculptors, and he did not begin to enjoy real professional success until the age of 40. His art 
30:693:55265:27:28:71:529:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:also developed slowly. Rainer Maria Rilke, who worked for several years as Rodin's secretary, 
30:694:55359:28:27:71:536:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:observed that “his work developed through long years. It has grown like a forest.”116 The reason 
30:695:55456:27:28:72:500:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:for this was clear to Rilke, for he explained that Rodin's art “depended upon an infallible 
30:696:55548:28:28:72:538:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:knowledge of the human body” that he acquired slowly and painstakingly: “His art was not built 
30:697:55643:28:27:72:524:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:upon a great idea, but upon a craft.”117 It was not only Rodin's craft that grew slowly, but also 
30:698:55741:27:28:72:532:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:individual works: “I am unfortunately a slow worker, being one of those artists in whose minds 
30:699:55836:28:27:72:444:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the conception of work slowly takes shape and slowly comes to maturity.”118 
30:700:55911:27:28:72:517:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Rodin's experimental art was avowedly visual: “I strive to express what I see with as 
30:701:55997:28:28:72:533:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:much deliberation as I can.”119   Ideas did not precede, but followed, form: “One must never try 
30:702:56094:28:27:72:528:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to express an idea by form. Make your form, make something, and the idea will come.”120 The 
30:703:56186:27:28:72:541:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:realism of Rodin’s figures was such that early in his career he was dogged by charges that he had 
30:704:56284:28:27:72:528:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:cast his sculptures from life. Infuriated by these rumors, he subsequently took care to make his 
30:705:56381:27:28:72:517:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:figures larger than life size. He did not work from imagination or memory, but always in the 
30:706:56474:28:27:72:542:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:presence of a model: he confessed that “I have no ideas when I don’t have something to copy.”121 
30:707:56570:27:28:71:524:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Unlike some sculptors who made figures that were intended to be seen only from the front, or 
30:708:56663:28:28:71:522:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:who might made a figure from a single front and single back view, Rodin created figures that 
30:709:56756:28:27:71:462:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:were to be seen in the round. His working process was iterative and incremental: 
30:710:56837:27:14:143:429:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: I place the model so that light, outlining it against a 
30:711:56893:14:14:143:444:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:background, illuminates the contour. I execute it, I change my 
30:712:56956:14:14:143:464:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:position and that of my model, and thus I see another contour, and 
30:713:57023:14:13:143:334:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:so on successively all around the body. 
30:714:57063:13:14:143:466:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: I begin again; I come closer and closer to the contours, and 
30:715:57124:14:14:143:211:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I refine them. 
30:716:57139:14:0:143:467:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Since the human body has an infinite number of contours, I 
31:717:57201:0:13:144:437:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:multiply them as far as possible or so far as I think useful.122 
31:718:57265:13:14:143:146:671:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
31:719:57265:14:28:71:529:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Rodin’s awareness of the body’s infinite number of contours makes it unsurprising that 
31:720:57352:28:27:71:504:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:he often had difficulties with completing his sculptures. He was incapable of planning his 
31:721:57443:27:28:71:514:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:projects in advance, and usually began his works without any clear conception of their final 
31:722:57536:28:28:71:538:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:appearance.123 He admitted that “I often begin with one intention and finish with another.”124 He 
31:723:57634:28:27:72:523:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:often reached an impasse and put a work aside for a time: “I lay my work aside while it is yet 
31:724:57729:27:28:72:538:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:unfinished, and for months I may appear to abandon it. Every now and then, however, I return to 
31:725:57825:28:27:72:523:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:it and correct or add a detail here and there. I have not really abandoned it, you see, only I am 
31:726:57923:27:28:72:532:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:hard to satisfy.”125 Nor did this process always result in a successful outcome, particularly with 
31:727:58022:28:28:71:531:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:large and complex works that would have benefited from careful planning. Thus George Heard 
31:728:58113:28:27:71:515:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Hamilton voiced a common criticism of Rodin when he remarked that he was “not strictly a 
31:729:58202:27:28:71:509:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:monumental sculptor, for he had difficulty in imposing a formal unity upon many separate 
31:730:58291:28:27:71:141:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:elements.”126  
31:731:58306:27:28:72:536:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Rodin in fact became known, by some admirers as well as many detractors, as a sculptor 
31:732:58393:28:27:72:518:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of unfinished works. In 1889 Edmond de Goncourt criticized Rodin’s figures for incomplete 
31:733:58483:27:28:72:530:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:execution, and compared his sculpture to recent developments in painting: “Amidst the present 
31:734:58577:28:28:72:533:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:infatuation with Impressionism, when all of painting remains in the sketch stage, [Rodin] ought 
31:735:58673:28:27:72:503:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to be the first to make his name and gloire as a sculptor of unfinished sketches.”127 Rodin 
31:736:58765:27:28:72:535:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:defended his fragmentary works against the charge that they were unfinished: “When my works 
31:737:58857:28:27:72:482:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:do not consist of the complete body ... people call it unfinished. What do they mean? 
31:738:58943:27:28:72:503:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Michelangelo’s finest works are precisely those which are called ‘unfinished.’”128 Rudolf 
31:739:59033:28:0:71:537:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Wittkower has argued that Rodin’s fragmentary sculptures were in fact finished: “The discovery 
32:740:59131:0:27:72:506:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that the part can stand for the whole was Rodin’s, and Brancusi along with scores of other 
32:741:59222:27:28:72:508:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sculptors accepted the premise. In contrast to Michelangelo, whose unfinished works were 
32:742:59311:28:27:72:523:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:unfinished, Rodin created partial figures which are the finished product.”129 Years of study of 
32:743:59407:27:28:72:530:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Rodin’s methods, however, led Albert Elsen to a different conclusion about both the fragments 
32:744:59501:28:28:71:512:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and the complete figures: “What now seems heroic and contemporary about Rodin is ... his 
32:745:59590:28:27:71:520:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:passion for the act of making rather than completing sculpture... His personal problem was in 
32:746:59684:27:28:71:523:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:setting for himself impossible absolutes of perfection toward which he dedicated a lifetime of 
32:747:59779:28:27:71:528:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:striving. It is doubtful that at his death he would have pronounced any of his works finished or 
32:748:59876:27:28:71:524:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:immutable.”130 Rodin himself recognized that he often sacrificed good works to the pursuit of 
32:749:59970:28:28:72:531:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:perfection: “Wishing to do better, one sometimes demolishes even what one has done well; but 
32:750:60063:28:27:72:325:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:one must be possessed by the demon of the best.”131 
32:751:60114:27:28:71:523:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Characteristically, Rodin produced the Monument to Balzac through a long process of 
32:752:60198:28:27:71:520:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:trial and error. Elsen’s analysis of the many works that made up this project prompted him to 
32:753:60292:27:28:71:528:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:observe that “One must look at the whole long series to see how slowly and naturally Balzac’s 
32:754:60386:28:27:71:528:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:posture and the position of his limbs evolved, rather than resulting from a sudden decision.”132 
32:755:60482:27:28:71:542:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Rodin failed to meet several deadlines for delivery of the statue, and on one of these occasions he 
32:756:60582:28:28:71:529:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:expressed his frustration: “As if it were possible, while one is searching, to be ready on a fixed 
32:757:60681:28:27:71:522:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:date!”133 Even when he finally delivered the work in 1898, Rodin would have liked to keep it 
32:758:60774:27:28:72:112:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:longer:  
32:759:60783:28:14:144:453:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:I should prefer to contemplate it every day for a while, and wait 
32:760:60849:14:13:143:466:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:until a sudden inspiration, such as occasionally flashes through the 
32:761:60918:13:14:143:463:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:brain, came to flood my imagination and enable me to perfect and 
32:762:60983:14:14:143:435:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:idealize my work. For a work, even when achieved, is never 
32:763:61042:14:0:143:465:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:perfect; it is always susceptible to a modification that can increase 
33:764:61115:0:13:144:208:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:its beauty.134 
33:765:61129:13:14:144:147:671:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
33:766:61129:14:28:72:537:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:            The            Monument to Balzac made striking use of Rodin’s central contributions to sculpture. 
33:767:61240:28:27:72:526:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Rodin wanted his sculptures to represent figures in movement, and he often animated them by 
33:768:61332:27:28:72:519:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:fixing transitory gestures and poses. Thus he portrayed Balzac not in formal dress or in quiet 
33:769:61427:28:28:72:533:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reflection, but instead in the monk’s robe he wore while working, in a dramatic stance, his head 
33:770:61524:28:27:72:309:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:thrown back in a moment of creative inspiration: 
33:771:61573:27:14:144:471:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:By convention, a statue in a pubic place must represent a great man 
33:772:61641:14:14:144:441:505:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in a theatrical attitude which will cause him to be admired by 
33:773:61704:14:14:144:452:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:posterity. But such reasoning is absurd. I submit there was only 
33:774:61769:14:13:144:455:477:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:one way to evoke my subject. I had to show Balzac in his study, 
33:775:61833:13:14:144:457:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:breathless, hair in disorder, eyes lost in a dream, a genius who in 
33:776:61901:14:14:144:464:450:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:his little room reconstructs piece by piece all of society in order to 
33:777:61972:14:14:144:427:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:bring it into tumultuous life before his contemporaries and 
33:778:62032:14:14:144:261:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:generations to come.135  
33:779:62057:14:13:144:147:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
33:780:62057:13:28:72:540:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Rodin was also concerned with creating atmospheric effects that would integrate his figures with 
33:781:62154:28:28:72:526:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:their environment. The jagged profile of Balzac, the deep cavities of the face and hair, and the 
33:782:62251:28:27:72:535:339:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:rough surface of the robe all created strong contrasts of light and shadow that called attention to 
33:783:62351:27:28:71:537:312:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the relationship between the figure and its surroundings. Throughout Rodin’s career he had used 
33:784:62447:28:27:72:527:284:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5:subjects that were not conventionally beautiful, but in the rough treatment of the Monument to 
33:785:62542:27:28:72:530:257:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Balzac he “created the first authentically ‘ugly’ work of modern art.”136 The effectiveness with 
33:786:62639:28:27:72:531:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:which Rodin combined these characteristic concerns and practices was a direct consequence of 
33:787:62732:27:28:72:520:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the maturity of this late stage of his career. As Rilke declared in tribute to his friend, “it is an 
33:788:62833:28:28:72:367:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:underlying patience in Rodin which renders him so great.”137 
33:789:62893:28:27:72:536:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Futurism was founded as a literary movement in 1908 by the Italian poet F. T. Marinetti, 
33:790:62982:27:0:71:517:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and the next year it was extended into painting, under the leadership of a young artist named 
34:791:63079:0:27:72:536:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Umberto Boccioni. In 1912 Boccioni decided to learn to make sculptures, and he devoted a year 
34:792:63173:27:28:72:523:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to that art before returning to painting. World War I effectively brought Futurism to an end in 
34:793:63269:28:27:72:527:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:1916, and in that year Boccioni was killed while serving in the Italian army. John Golding has 
34:794:63364:27:28:72:502:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:observed that in its brief career Futurist visual art produced a single major masterpiece, a 
34:795:63457:28:28:72:536:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sculpture by Boccioni titled Unique Forms of Continuity in Space.138 A recent survey confirmed 
34:796:63552:28:27:72:540:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Golding’s judgment, finding that this sculpture is illustrated in more textbooks of art history than 
34:797:63653:27:28:72:523:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:any single work by such great modern sculptors as Rodin, Henry Moore, Alberto Giacometti, 
34:798:63743:28:27:72:529:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and David Smith.139 Remarkably, therefore, although Boccioni’s total production of sculptures 
34:799:63837:27:28:72:540:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:has been estimated at just a dozen, one of these, which he executed at the age of 31, is among the 
34:800:63936:28:28:72:293:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:most celebrated sculptures of the modern era. 
34:801:63982:28:27:72:533:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Futurism was a conceptual movement from the outset. Marinetti stated one of its central 
34:802:64070:27:28:72:528:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:themes in his first Futurist manifesto in 1909 when he declared that “the world’s magnificence 
34:803:64165:28:27:72:503:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:has been enriched by a new beauty: the beauty of speed.”140 A central concern of Futurist 
34:804:64255:27:28:72:518:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:painting would consequently be the representation of motion. When Boccioni and four other 
34:805:64345:28:27:72:538:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:painters joined the movement, they explained that they wanted not to portray “a fixed moment in 
34:806:64441:27:28:72:517:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:universal dynamism,” but rather “the dynamic sensation itself”; to represent movement over 
34:807:64532:28:28:72:536:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:time, they intended to create visual syntheses of “what one remembers and of what one sees.”141 
34:808:64627:28:27:72:533:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: A central feature of Futurism was its self-consciously revolutionary attitude. Marinetti’s 
34:809:64718:27:28:72:534:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:founding statement in 1909 described itself as a “violently upsetting incendiary manifesto,” and 
34:810:64815:28:27:72:519:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the painters who joined him the next year opened their first manifesto with a declaration that 
34:811:64910:27:28:72:543:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:theirs was a “cry of rebellion.”142 A closely associated theme was the youth of the participants, as 
34:812:65011:28:0:72:532:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Marinetti’s first manifesto remarked that “the oldest of us is thirty, so we have at least a decade 
35:813:65114:0:27:72:444:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:for finishing our work” before they would be surpassed by younger artists.143 
35:814:65191:27:28:72:538:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In a number of respects Futurism became a prototype for later art movements, perhaps as 
35:815:65279:28:27:72:532:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:much for its novel practices as for its art. Malevich and many other advanced artists learned the 
35:816:65377:27:28:72:492:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Futurist lesson that written texts could be a valuable tool in expanding the audience for 
35:817:65467:28:28:72:525:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:innovative conceptual art. In less than a decade Futurist artists produced dozens of pamphlets, 
35:818:65563:28:27:72:533:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:usually specifically titled as manifestos, on subjects ranging from poetry and painting to theater 
35:819:65662:27:28:72:496:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and film, and even to new forms of men’s clothing.144 Historians have observed that the 
35:820:65750:28:27:72:540:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Futurists’ publication of manifestos typically preceded the execution of the relevant works of art, 
35:821:65850:27:28:72:507:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and have remarked that this effectively made the manifestos blueprints for works of art.145 
35:822:65941:28:28:71:497:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Marjorie Perloff also made the observation that with this practice the Futurists made the 
35:823:66031:28:27:71:527:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:manifestos a part of their art: “to talk about art becomes equivalent to making it.”146 It appears 
35:824:66130:27:28:72:495:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that the influence of Futurism was increased not only by the function of written texts as 
35:825:66220:28:27:72:506:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:supporting documents for works of art, but in some cases directly by the texts, even in the 
35:826:66312:27:28:71:536:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:absence of associated works of art.147 The texts could in fact have an impact even on artists who 
35:827:66410:28:27:72:531:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:rejected the Futurists’ art. So for example in 1912 Franz Marc wrote to his friend Kandinsky of 
35:828:66506:27:28:72:526:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the Futurists that “I cannot free myself from the strange contradiction that I find their ideas, at 
35:829:66606:28:28:72:514:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:least for the main part, brilliant, but am in no doubt whatsoever as to the mediocrity of their 
35:830:66702:28:27:72:124:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:works.”148 
35:831:66712:27:28:72:503:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In 1909 Boccioni was an ambitious young painter working in a conventional Post-
35:832:66791:28:27:72:531:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Impressionist style when he met the dynamic Marinetti, who described himself as the “caffeine 
35:833:66885:27:28:72:534:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of Europe.”149 Marinetti inspired Boccioni, and proceeded to “drag him, technically unprepared 
35:834:66980:28:0:72:513:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and with a torrent of only partially formulated ideas, into the mainstream of modern art.”150 
36:835:67076:0:27:72:526:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Boccioni quickly adopted a highly conceptual approach to art. Thus he wrote to a friend that a 
36:836:67171:27:28:72:535:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:new painting was “done completely without models, and all the tricks of the trade are sacrificed 
36:837:67268:28:27:72:523:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to the ultimate cause of emotional expression ... [T]he emotion will be presented with as little 
36:838:67365:27:28:72:376:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:recourse as possible to the objects that have given rise to it.”151 
36:839:67432:28:28:72:525:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Boccioni and the other Futurist painters aimed to portray motion as a dynamic process 
36:840:67518:28:27:71:541:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that occurred and was experienced over time. They also wanted to represent the tendency of both 
36:841:67614:27:28:71:526:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:light and motion to destroy the concreteness of forms, thus emphasizing the interplay between 
36:842:67708:28:27:71:524:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:objects and their environment.152 Late in 1911 Boccioni spent a month in Paris, where he saw 
36:843:67801:27:28:72:539:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:new Cubist techniques that he quickly adapted to Futurist ends in his paintings.153 While in Paris 
36:844:67900:28:28:72:534:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Boccioni appears to have become aware that there was not yet a Cubist school of sculpture, and 
36:845:67995:28:27:72:526:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that sculpture had consequently lagged behind painting in the development of advanced art. A 
36:846:68088:27:28:72:534:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:consequence of this was that he might make an immediate impact on the art world by extending 
36:847:68181:28:27:72:528:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the concerns of Futurist art to sculpture.154 In March of 1912 Boccioni wrote to a friend that “I 
36:848:68279:27:28:72:541:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:am obsessed these days by sculpture. I think I can perceive a complete revival of this mummified 
36:849:68376:28:27:72:534:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:art.”155 The next month he published a manifesto proposing a Futurist sculpture that would both 
36:850:68472:27:28:72:478:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:represent movement and portray the interaction of the figure and its environment.156 
36:851:68556:28:28:72:535:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Boccioni then began to make sculptures.157 In June of 1913 he exhibited 11 sculptures at 
36:852:68645:28:27:72:515:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:a Paris gallery. Guillaume Apollinaire’s review praised his achievement: “Varied materials, 
36:853:68737:27:28:72:534:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sculptural simultaneity, violent movement - these are the innovations contributed by Boccioni’s 
36:854:68833:28:27:72:495:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5:sculpture.” Apollinaire closed the review with a facetious reference to Unique Forms of 
36:855:68921:27:28:72:519:133:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Continuity in Space: “Flash: We have been informed that Boccioni’s “muscles at full speed” 
36:856:69012:28:0:72:441:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:have taken to the road. As of this writing, they have not been recaptured.”158 
37:857:69093:0:27:72:541:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:            In            Unique Forms, the surfaces of an advancing figure are broken into parts, but instead of 
37:858:69207:27:28:72:537:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the straight lines and sharp angles of Cubism they have been transformed into irregularly curved 
37:859:69304:28:27:72:530:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:planes that blend together in a graceful composition. Their orientation and aerodynamic shapes 
37:860:69399:27:28:72:536:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:create forms that appear bulky and muscular at the same time that they seem to flow in response 
37:861:69495:28:28:72:522:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:to strong winds blowing in the face of the figure. In this sculpture Boccioni produced a novel 
37:862:69590:28:27:72:510:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:synthesis, drawing on plastic forms taken from classical Greek sculpture and techniques of 
37:863:69681:27:28:72:519:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:spatial organization borrowed from advanced modern painting, to create a three-dimensional 
37:864:69772:28:27:72:531:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:representation of the effects, in both vision and memory, of a combination of power and speed. 
37:865:69867:27:28:72:495:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:            Boccioni            considered            Unique Forms “the most liberated” of his sculptures.159 He 
37:866:69980:28:28:72:490:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:apparently gave up sculpting after he executed it, and Golding concluded that “with its 
37:867:70068:28:27:72:534:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:completion, Boccioni seems to have realized that he had achieved the definitive masterpiece for 
37:868:70164:27:28:72:529:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:which he longed.”160 Not only did Boccioni succeed in making a major contribution to modern 
37:869:70256:28:27:72:517:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:sculpture at the age of just 31, but he did it just a year after making his first sculptures. Even 
37:870:70355:27:28:72:538:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:then, he believed that he could have achieved much more, and at an earlier age, under the proper 
37:871:70452:28:27:72:539:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:circumstances. Thus when he reported to a friend Apollinaire’s praise for his 1913 exhibition, he 
37:872:70550:27:28:72:534:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:added that in spite of his excitement at his success, “I end up sad and discouraged. I think about 
37:873:70649:28:28:72:527:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:what I would have done by now if I had grown up with Paris or Berlin as my environment.”161 
37:874:70740:28:27:71:132:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Playwrights 
37:875:70751:27:14:144:368:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:My task has been the description of humanity. 
37:876:70797:14:14:143:395:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Henrik            Ibsen,            1898162 
37:877:70888:14:14:144:147:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
37:878:70888:14:13:144:464:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:You are free to see in Monsieur Ubu all the multiple allusions you 
37:879:70955:13:14:144:469:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:wish, or a simple puppet, the deformation by a schoolboy of one of 
37:880:71022:14:14:144:450:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:his teachers who represented for him all that is grotesque in the 
37:881:71088:14:0:144:178:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:world. 
38:882:71098:0:13:144:461:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:                                                Alfred            Jarry,            December            10,            1896163 
38:883:71224:13:14:143:146:671:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
38:884:71224:14:28:71:530:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: A recent survey called Hedda Gabler “perhaps the most perfectly structured play of the 
38:885:71311:28:27:71:531:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:modern theater.”164 It was written by Henrik Ibsen in 1890, when he was 62 years old, and was 
38:886:71405:27:28:72:532:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:one of a series of plays that made Ibsen the preeminent dramatist of the late nineteenth century. 
38:887:71503:28:28:72:540:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Ibsen is commonly described as the founder of the modern theater.165 He is celebrated for 
38:888:71593:28:27:71:515:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the innovation of the realistic drama. Ibsen once explained that “My object was to make the 
38:889:71685:27:28:71:509:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:reader feel that he was going through a piece of real experience.”166 George Bernard Shaw 
38:890:71775:28:27:72:535:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:observed that when Ibsen began writing, the theater dealt largely with contrived situations, “and 
38:891:71873:27:28:72:529:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:it was held that the stranger the situation, the better the play.” Ibsen changed this: “He gives us 
38:892:71973:28:28:72:509:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not only ourselves, but ourselves in our own situations. The things that happen to his stage 
38:893:72066:28:27:72:528:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:figures are things that happen to us.”167 In his first published article James Joyce had made the 
38:894:72164:27:28:71:517:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:same observation: “Ibsen has chosen the average lives in their uncompromising truth for the 
38:895:72256:28:27:72:251:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:groundwork of all his later plays.”168 
38:896:72294:27:28:72:540:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Ibsen achieved this realism by beginning with his characters: “Always I proceed from the 
38:897:72383:28:27:72:535:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:individuals; the stage-setting, the dramatic ensemble, all that comes naturally, and causes me no 
38:898:72481:27:28:72:534:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:worry, once I feel sure of the individual in every aspect of his humanity.”169 Once Ibsen had his 
38:899:72579:28:28:72:520:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:characters in mind, writing a play was a process of getting to know them, and he did this in a 
38:900:72674:28:27:72:151:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:series of stages: 
38:901:72692:27:14:144:442:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:As a rule, I make three drafts of my dramas which differ very 
38:902:72754:14:14:144:452:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:much from each other in characterization, not in action. When I 
38:903:72818:14:14:143:466:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:proceed to the first sketch of the material I feel as though I had the 
38:904:72889:14:13:144:459:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:degree of acquaintance with my characters that one acquires on a 
38:905:72954:13:14:144:433:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:railway journey... With the next draft I see everything more 
38:906:73015:14:14:144:462:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:clearly, I know the characters just about as one would know them 
38:907:73080:14:0:143:452:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:after a few weeks’ stay in a spa; I have learned the fundamental 
39:908:73148:0:13:144:462:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:traits in their characters as well as their little peculiarities; yet it is 
39:909:73224:13:14:144:468:671:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:not impossible that I might make an error in some essential matter. 
39:910:73292:14:14:144:456:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:In the last draft, finally I stand at the limit of knowledge; I know 
39:911:73361:14:14:144:461:643:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:my people from close and long association - they are my intimate 
39:912:73426:14:14:144:388:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:friends, who will not disappoint me in any way.170 
39:913:73476:14:13:144:147:615:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
39:914:73476:13:28:72:537:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ibsen developed his characters during this process not only through their speeches, but also their 
39:915:73575:28:28:72:526:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:actions: “Many of the details occur to me during the process of composition, bit by bit as I get 
39:916:73672:28:27:72:536:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:along.”171 Their physical appearance could also change, for Ibsen had to visualize the characters 
39:917:73770:27:28:72:514:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:as he wrote: “To write is to see.”172 In view of the central role of characterization in Ibsen’s 
39:918:73867:28:27:72:518:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:process of composition, it is not surprising that Henry James would predict that Ibsen would 
39:919:73960:27:28:72:534:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:always be loved by actors: “He cuts them out work to which the artistic nature in them joyously 
39:920:74056:28:28:72:535:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:responds - work difficult and interesting, full of stuff and opportunity.”173 Just a few years later, 
39:921:74158:28:27:71:542:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:a leading actor confirmed James’ view: “It is easier, I think, to get inside the skin of an Ibsen part 
39:922:74261:27:28:71:518:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:than any other, for the simple reason that the characterization is so minute and elaborate, the 
39:923:74357:28:27:71:507:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:words are so full of suggestion, that the actor has infinite scope for the exercise of his best 
39:924:74453:27:28:71:136:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:qualities.”174 
39:925:74467:28:27:72:531:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: The primary importance of characterization points to the experimental nature of Ibsen’s 
39:926:74555:27:28:72:529:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:art, as does another central feature of his plays, their focus on change. Arthur Miller concluded 
39:927:74653:28:28:71:521:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that “If his plays, and his method, do nothing else they reveal the evolutionary quality of life. 
39:928:74751:28:27:71:504:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:One is constantly aware, in watching his plays, of process, change, development.”175 Eric 
39:929:74841:27:28:72:542:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Bentley explained that this awareness was a product of Ibsen’s method of exposition: “Generally, 
39:930:74938:28:27:72:542:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:with Ibsen, we feel we are his companions in a search and therefore... are not given summaries of 
39:931:75036:27:28:72:514:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:what has been thought already but are present at the thinking.”176 In his major late plays the 
39:932:75131:28:0:72:524:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:endings were often not fully resolved, reflecting “a conception of life which Ibsen considered 
40:933:75229:0:27:72:534:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:more true than that represented by the stage convention of a conclusively happy or tragic end as 
40:934:75326:27:28:72:530:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the curtain falls.”177 Elizabeth Hardwick observed that in Ibsen’s late plays “His people are not 
40:935:75424:28:27:72:529:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:fixed. They are growing, moving, uncertain of their direction in life.” She traced this quality to 
40:936:75523:27:28:72:510:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:an uncertainty in the playwright: “We feel Ibsen himself created certain characters out of a 
40:937:75616:28:28:72:326:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:musing wonder and deep, intriguing uncertainty.”178 
40:938:75667:28:27:72:516:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f5: Ibsen developed his mature art in an extended series of major works, ranging from A 
40:939:75751:27:28:72:521:519:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f5:Doll’s House (1879) and The Wild Duck (1884) through Hedda Gabler (1890) to The Master 
40:940:75838:28:27:72:525:491:144:g_d0_f5:144:g_d0_f4:Builder (1892) and John Gabriel Borkman (1896). All of these plays embodied his distinctive 
40:941:75930:27:28:71:528:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:innovations, including the use of discussion as a primary focus of interest, and the portrayal of 
40:942:76028:28:28:71:517:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:realistic characters, and actions, that had the effect of “making the spectators themselves the 
40:943:76124:28:27:71:532:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:persons of the dramas.”179 The plays’ subject matter directly treated major social issues of their 
40:944:76223:27:28:72:496:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:time. True to his experimental nature, Ibsen’s work did not change abruptly but evolved 
40:945:76311:28:27:71:540:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:gradually: “Ibsen chose the path of constant development ... Not the smallest fascination of Ibsen 
40:946:76410:27:28:71:460:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:is the unity of his work, the profound meaning in the relation of play to play.”180 
40:947:76493:28:27:72:511:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: No single play dominates Ibsen’s career, but Hedda Gabler was among his greatest 
40:948:76574:27:28:72:540:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:achievements. In a contemporary review, Henry James remarked that the study of an exasperated 
40:949:76668:28:28:72:535:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:woman would not have seemed a promising subject, but that the play demonstrated “the folly of 
40:950:76762:28:27:71:539:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:quarreling with an artist over his subject. Ibsen has had only to take hold of this one in earnest to 
40:951:76864:27:28:71:540:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:make it, against every presumption, live with an intensity of life.”181 For many critics Hedda was 
40:952:76963:28:27:72:504:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ibsen’s greatest character; thus Harold Bloom declared that “What Anna Karenina was to 
40:953:77050:27:28:72:528:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Tolstoy, and Emma Bovary to Flaubert, Hedda was to Ibsen.”182 Elizabeth Hardwick observed 
40:954:77140:28:0:72:525:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:that “Hedda Gabler challenges and pleases and is the most fascinating, humanly interesting of 
41:955:77237:0:27:72:539:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ibsen’s women. Actresses long to play the role.”183 John Northam explained that Ibsen’s success 
41:956:77333:27:28:72:533:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in this characterization was a result of his subtle use of his full experience as a playwright: “our 
41:957:77434:28:27:71:520:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:informative details have been infinitely small, and ordinary; but they have been organized so 
41:958:77528:27:28:71:503:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:precisely, into patterns so self-consistent that through them we have become aware of the 
41:959:77618:28:28:72:539:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:development of a character whose main failing was inarticulateness... Through visual suggestion 
41:960:77714:28:27:71:539:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:we have come to know the stifling pressures and the fierce reaction... We have been reached, not 
41:961:77811:27:28:71:532:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:by the novelist’s techniques operating solely through the mind, but through the eyes; never was 
41:962:77907:28:27:72:475:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ibsen more a practicing playwright than he was when he created Hedda Gabler.”184 
41:963:77987:27:28:72:534:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Ibsen’s innovations made his plays extremely controversial in his own time. The loudest 
41:964:78075:28:28:72:539:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:complaints came from those who believed that the theater should provide amusing entertainment 
41:965:78169:28:27:72:494:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:in ways that would not challenge prevailing social values. These complaints most often 
41:966:78256:27:28:72:511:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:denounced the ugliness of Ibsen’s subjects. So for example one London critic declared that 
41:967:78347:28:27:72:522:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:“Hedda Gabler is the study of a malicious woman of evil instincts, jealous, treacherous, cold-
41:968:78441:27:28:71:537:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:hearted, and, as it seems to us, wholly out of place on the stage,” while another reflected that “to 
41:969:78542:28:27:71:527:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:conceive of the Ibsen drama gaining an extensive or permanent foothold on the stage is hardly 
41:970:78636:27:28:72:514:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:possible. Playgoing would then cease to be an amusement and become a penance.”185 Other 
41:971:78724:28:28:72:533:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:critics complained that their evening’s entertainment lacked any edifying resolution: “If [Ibsen] 
41:972:78822:28:27:72:536:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:is a prophet, he is one who has no mission, no message to give us ... He only puts the destroying 
41:973:78920:27:28:72:530:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:question; never does he furnish even a hint of the saving answer.”186 Yet to many young artists 
41:974:79016:28:27:72:514:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ibsen’s dramas were intensely exciting, as they laid the foundations for a theater that would 
41:975:79110:27:28:72:524:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:explore important social and psychological problems, and in so doing become a more integral 
41:976:79202:28:0:72:502:105:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:part of modern intellectual life. In recognition of this Luigi Pirandello, one of the leading 
42:977:79299:0:27:72:524:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:playwrights of the next generation, declared that “After Shakespeare, without hesitation, I put 
42:978:79395:27:28:72:146:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ibsen first.”187  
42:979:79413:28:27:72:526:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: In 1961, a critic named Martin Esslin published a book defining a movement he called 
42:980:79498:27:28:72:539:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:the Theater of the Absurd, which included such playwrights as Samuel Beckett, Eugène Ionesco, 
42:981:79592:28:28:71:540:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Jean Genet, and Harold Pinter. Esslin declared that this theater “strives to express its sense of the 
42:982:79694:28:27:71:526:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:senselessness of the human condition and the inadequacy of the rational approach by the open 
42:983:79787:27:28:71:514:519:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:abandonment of rational devices and discursive thought.” Esslin dated the beginning of this 
42:984:79879:28:27:71:530:491:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:movement very precisely, to the evening of December 10, 1896, when a Paris theater company 
42:985:79970:27:28:71:350:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:first presented Ubu Roi, a play written by Alfred Jarry.188 
42:986:80029:28:28:72:538:436:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Jarry was 23 years old when Ubu Roi opened in Paris. The origins of the work went back 
42:987:80116:28:27:72:540:408:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:eight years, to when the 15-year-old Jarry and two school friends in a provincial lycée first wrote 
42:988:80216:27:28:72:516:381:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:a puppet play about their physics teacher. The obese and incompetent teacher had long been 
42:989:80307:28:27:72:513:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:ridiculed by his students, and Jarry and his friends drew on a rich oral tradition in satirizing 
42:990:80404:27:28:72:528:326:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Monsieur Hébert, whose name had variously been transformed into Heb, Hébé, and eventually 
42:991:80494:28:27:72:509:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ubu. Unlike his classmates, Jarry persisted in developing the epic history of the imaginary 
42:992:80586:27:28:72:285:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:adventures of Ubu even after he left school. 
42:993:80631:28:28:72:538:243:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: At 17 Jarry moved to Paris, where he soon abandoned his plans to attend college in favor 
42:994:80720:28:27:72:519:215:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of becoming a writer. He became part of a literary group that included the leading Symbolist 
42:995:80813:27:28:72:537:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:poets and novelists of the time, and began to publish stories about Ubu and his supposed science 
42:996:80910:28:27:72:489:160:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:of “ ́Pataphysicks.” Although Jarry’s language was often bombastic, he in fact worked 
42:997:80996:27:28:72:346:133:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:methodically in developing  ́Pataphysicks into a system:

43:1003:81295:14:14:144:147:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: 
43:1004:81295:14:27:72:532:615:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Jarry wanted to create a theater purely of the imagination, that made no appeal to facts or logic. 
43:1005:81394:27:28:72:510:588:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:As a recent biographer explained, in Jarry’s conception “theater should imitate the logic of 
43:1006:81487:28:27:72:524:560:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:dreams, and should therefore employ symbolism, condensation, disconnection of images, and 
43:1007:81577:27:28:72:497:533:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:displacement; it should have an elusive, ephemeral and haunting quality.”190  
43:1008:81655:28:28:72:520:505:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:            In            Ubu Roi, the greedy and cowardly Ubu becomes King of Poland, tortures and kills 
43:1009:81761:28:27:72:521:477:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:many of his subjects, and is ultimately chased out of the country. The monstrous character of 
43:1010:81855:27:28:72:524:450:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:Ubu drew liberally upon that of Falstaff and the inspiration of Rabelais, and the staging of the 
43:1011:81952:28:27:72:542:422:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:play was based on the marionette forms in which Jarry had first performed it. When Ubu Roi was 
43:1012:82047:27:28:72:528:395:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:first presented to the public in 1896, the play had been widely advertised and its opening night 
43:1013:82144:28:28:72:529:367:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:was attended by many of Paris’ leading critics and eminent literary figures. Controversy began 
43:1014:82239:28:27:72:528:339:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:immediately. When the curtain was raised, the actor portraying the gross Ubu stepped forward 
43:1015:82332:27:28:72:517:312:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:and spoke his first line, a common obscenity that Jarry had appropriated to  ́Pataphysicks by 
43:1016:82426:28:27:72:504:284:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:adding a single letter. The audience erupted into shouting, whistling, and even fighting as 
43:1017:82518:27:28:72:522:257:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:admirers and detractors expressed their approval, shock, and anger. The actors had to wait 15 
43:1018:82612:28:27:72:540:229:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:minutes before the play could resume, and many more interruptions followed in the course of the 
43:1019:82708:27:28:72:128:202:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:evening.191 
43:1020:82719:28:28:72:517:174:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Ubu Roi was given a second performance the next evening, but then closed, and was 
43:1021:82801:28:27:71:497:146:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:never again performed in Jarry’s lifetime. Yet the impact of its opening night gave Jarry 
43:1022:82891:27:0:71:542:119:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:immediate fame in literary circles, as for weeks afterward favorable and unfavorable views of the 
44:1023:82992:0:27:72:539:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:play were debated in Paris’ major newspapers. This proved to be the high point of Jarry’s career. 
44:1024:83090:27:28:72:512:657:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:He continued to write about Ubu, and over time began to assume the character’s manner of 
44:1025:83179:28:27:72:478:629:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:speaking. Not unlike Rimbaud, Jarry attempted to stimulate his imagination through 
44:1026:83262:27:28:72:341:602:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:hallucination, which eventually proved self-destructive. 
44:1027:83319:28:28:72:518:574:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Roger Shattuck observed that “at thirty Jarry had completed his best years, enjoyed a 
44:1028:83405:28:27:72:485:546:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:unique notoriety in the literary world of Paris, and was already looked up to by a new

44:1031:83680:27:28:72:533:464:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:eccentricities, even, after Jarry’s death, carrying around the gun that Jarry had always kept with

44:1035:84066:28:27:72:497:353:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:traditional standards of beauty and good taste, but even more specifically by a particular

44:1037:84257:28:27:72:515:298:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:multiple viewpoints of Cubism in a  ́Pataphysickal treatise of 1898: “to claim the shape of a 
44:1038:84351:27:28:72:499:271:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:watch is round [is] a manifestly false proposition - since it appears in profile as a narrow

44:1041:84594:27:28:71:517:188:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4: Jarry died at the age of just 34, of tuberculosis aggravated by his drinking. His major

45:1045:84964:0:27:72:534:684:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:have done it as a sure, sober, dramatic sculptor. He has joined the repertory of the best taste and
